M.Sc. Thesis
Master of Science in Engineering
BIM, Linked Data and The Semantic Web
An investigation of automated CCS classification
using linked semantic relations
Abderrahim Kazhaze (s113981)
Arbaz Hamayun (s112449)
Kongens Lyngby 2017
==PAGE:2==
DTU Civil Engineering
Department of Civil Engineering
Technical University of Denmark
Brovej
Building 118

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This thesis presents and states an approach on how to utilize Linked Data and Semantic
Web technologies and principles to automate CCS classification and thereby optimize
BIM related tasks. A qualitative survey focusing on the usage of classification systems
within consulting-company ALECTIA outlined the issues and challenges related to the
manual and iterative work. The issues mainly consisted of the lack of consistency within
CCS classification and CCS identification codes for usage in tender documents, which
resulted in time consumption and manual assignment of identification codes.
Combining the acquired knowledge about RDF syntax and OWL logic a partial CCS
ontology was developed, where CCS classes were mapped to IFC entities. By the uti-
lization of the semantic relations in IFC it was possible to develop rulesets specifically
for the determination of CCS multi-level-, location-, and functional IDs. The implemen-
tation of a converted Autodesk Revit model to an ifcOWL-based IFC graph-model, the
developed CCS ontology and rulesets in a graph-database made it possible to reason
and query CCS entities. A back-end application server was then created collaborating
with the graph-database paving the way for an automatic computing and a successfully
extraction of CCS classification and identification codes.
Although the product have generated the CCS classification and identifications automati-
cally the solution is still limited due to the changing - and iterative nature of the current
BIM-related tasks. The product relies on a static tool, converting the IFC model to
RDF, resulting in static classification and identification codes depending on the conver-
sion of IFC to RDF. Furthermore the limited entity definitions in IFC does not facilitate
proper mapping from CCS to IFC. Moreover property values in BIM models and IFC
needs standardization and extension in order to capture more semantic definitions and
allowing for better reasoning of CCS elements and components.
We believe that further development within collaboration of BIM and Semantic Web
technologies together with the ifcOWL ontology would facilitate more efficient and au-
tomated classification and identification of construction-related data. Furthermore we
see a potential for more sustainable collaboration and design within the AEC industry
utilizing the potential of this technology.
However this requires further understand-
ing of domain-specific information and implementation of such semantically supported
work-flows in the sotfware-platforms.

==PAGE:5==
Preface
This paper is the result of the authors thesis-project prepared at the department of Civil
Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The thesis is written in fulfillment
of the requirements for acquiring a Master of Science in Engineering degree in Civil
Engineering.
The course of the thesis spanned from September 12, 2016 to April 16, 2017. It repre-
sents 30 ECTS points from both authors.
The main topic of the thesis was the application of Semantic Web and Linked Data prin-
ciples to BIM models and processes. The aim of the thesis was to utilize this combination
of technologies to investigate the possibilities of automating building classification and
more specifically CCS classification.
We would like to thank our supervisors Jan Karlshøj and Mads Holten Rasmussen for
thorough and qualified supervision, assistance and comments. Furthermore we want to
thank Asger Wahlquist Petersen for sharing his and ALECTIA’s knowledge about and
experience with BIM projects and CCS utilization. Finally we would like to thank our
family and friends for supporting us through our thesis and studies.

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
1.1
Problem definition
In recent years the use of Building Information Modeling (BIM) as a collaboration plat-
form for the Architecture, Engineering and Construction (AEC) domain has increased
significantly (Eastman et al. 2011). The AEC domain has been notorious for its ineffi-
ciency with regards to data exchange and management primarily due to the fragmented
environment of collaboration and also the use of non-digital tools for representation and
exchange of data (Eastman et al. 2011). The growing implementations of BIM supported
workflows has yielded innovations in this regard with the information of a building in
a digital format, which are semantically richer and better exchangeable (Pauwels and
Terkaj 2016).
By the nature of projects within the AEC domain several different knowledge bases
are represented creating a multidisciplinary environment. With the increasing use of
BIM across these different domains, specific applications supporting the tasks of each
knowledge base are developed as a result (Costa and Pauwels 2015). Essential informa-
tion for each knowledge base is therefore stored in these specific applications and data
formats creating heterogeneous information sources (Beetz, Leeuwen, and Vries 2009).
This poses a challenge with decoupling information, maintaining data consistency, and
sharing information across the project domain.
To facilitate the collaboration and automation of BIM data file exchanges across
disciplines the international organization BuildingSMART developed the object ori-
ented data model standard Industry Foundation Classes (IFC) (BuildingSMART 2016a).
Through the introduction of the IFC standard designers and engineers are able to de-
scribe construction-related information that is well-defined and interlinked through rela-
tional constraints (Pauwels et al. 2011). Several use cases and investigations have shown
certain limitations with IFC in regards to the interoperability of information between
different domains, and also with capturing the knowledge and experiences of domain
experts (Costa and Pauwels 2015), (Zhang and Beetz 2015), (Venugopal et al. 2012),
(Pauwels et al. 2011).
Linked Data and Semantic Web technologies have been proposed as a solution to
mitigate these challenges and limitations (Zhang and Beetz 2016), (Costa and Pauwels
2015), (Pauwels and Terkaj 2016). The Semantic Web provides a logic-based and generic
language that is aimed to be understood by computers and applications with the goal
of combining and sharing knowledge from various different domains by creating links
between data (Berners-Lee, Hendler, and Lassila 2001). This is done using different on-
==PAGE:20==
2
1 Introduction
tology representations of information, where each ontology represents a specific domain
and adds contextualization to the data. In relation to BIM and information-exchanges
one such ontology representation has been researched and developed over recent years
for the IFC format called ifcOWL.
One area of importance with increasing demands of systematization is the classifi-
cation and identification of construction-related information such as building elements
and processes. Several systems are available and widespread for these tasks such as
OmniClass, CCS or SfB. CCS operates with both classification and identification on
multiple levels corresponding to product-, type-, multi-level-, location-, and functional-
IDs. These properties are to be determined on the basis of the type of elements, their
properties and the relationships between the elements within the building. Utilizing the
IFC schema for these tasks seems natural, since this data standard provides relational
data and constraints of entities. Furthermore developing and transforming CCS to an
equivalent ontology by utilizing ifcOWL and the semantic web technologies promises to
automate the processes of CCS classification and identification.
This thesis intents to investigate the potentials of utilizing Linked Data and Seman-
tic Web technologies and more specifically the development of ifcOWL. Based upon this
investigation an equivalent ontology for CCS is to be developed for automation of CCS
classification and identification utilizing the potential within BIM. This has resulted in
the following problem statement:
How can Linked Data and Semantic Web technologies be implemented and utilized
for purposes related to the AEC industry? How can CCS be represented as an
equivalent ontology? How can the utilization of the CCS ontology in combination
with ifcOWL automate the classification and identification of BIM models?
1.2
Methodology
To answer the research questions a well-established understanding of Linked Data and
Semantic Web technologies was needed. In addition to this comprehending the relation of
classification systems with BIM projects and processes was of importance for establishing
an application for automating the use of CCS based on semantic technologies.
1.2.1
Literature study
A three-part literature study was conducted covering the themes (1) the current practices
and specifications designed for optimizing BIM workflows and the definitions and prac-
tices of the CCS classification system, (2) Linked Data and Semantic Web technologies,
logic and implementation, and (3) the developments within building-domain ontologies
and the representation of IFC in an OWL format. In addition to the literature study an
online web-course with title ’Linked Data Engineering’ conducted at the Hasso-Platner
Institut was attended. The motivation for attending this course was that this thesis
required that the authors learned a new data format and language namely RDF and
==PAGE:21==
1.2 Methodology
3
OWL, which was covered among other technologies in this course. The findings of the
three-parted literature study created the foundation for development of a CCS ontology
and related rulesets for reasoning of CCS classification and identification on the basis of
semantic relations of building model elements.
1.2.2
Qualitative survey
In addition to understanding the CCS system and the relevance of implementing Linked
Data and Semantic Web principles, the current use of classification systems in the AEC
industry was of interest and importance. Therefore a qualitative survey was prepared
and conducted with consulting-company ALECTIA in order to grasp the utilization and
experiences with CCS. The participants chosen to be interviewed had professional roles
BIM-supervisor and HVAC-engineer. Prior to the survey a stage-divided interview guide
was developed with each stage covering an increasing taxonomic level.
1.2.3
Product development
For modelling of a test BIM model the software Autodesk Revit was utilized.
The
product concerns itself with IFC models and hence other BIM software supporting IFC
export are also compatible in relation to the product and product-flow. In order to
generate and convert the IFC model to a RDF-based dataset a demo conversion tool
was used. For storing, querying and reasoning RDF datasets on the basis of ontologies
and rulesets a graph-database software was needed. Stardog was utilized for this purpose
and hence developing a product based on this graph-database also required education
of creating datasets, utilizing the reasoner, and querying data.
In order to compute IDs and display results of the CCS coding a back-end application
was developed using JavaScript in the cloud-environment called Cloud9. This served as
a user-interface where the user would recieve results by entering a specific URL on
the basis of the CCS ontology, rulesets, reasoning in Stardog, and computation by the
Cloud9 server.
1.2.4
Points of critique
The used approach is restricted to a single consulting-company’s use of CCS. To get a
broader understanding of the use of classification systems in the Danish AEC domain a
quantitative industry survey would have been more appropriate, where other domains
could have shared experiences and reflections related to the work related to the classifi-
cation systems in general.
Furthermore is the approach focusing on CCS. A focus on a wider spectrum of the
usage of classification systems in general would have given a more holistic result.
The scope of the methodology is limited to a sub-part of the CCS, namely the
classification and identification. In order to make use of the full potential of CCS the
==PAGE:22==
4
1 Introduction
other aspects, such as attributes/properties could have been included. Although the
IFC format is in focus the entire approach relies on the compatibility of Autodesk Revit
with IFC. An inclusion of other BIM tools such as ArchiCAD, Tekla etc. would have
proven the concepts more optimal.
==PAGE:23==
CHAPTER 2
BIM workflow and
standards
This chapter of the thesis presents the concepts and findings of the first part of the
literature study.
The initial sections cover the current practice within the AEC do-
main related to the tasks of design and data management in a BIM oriented work flow.
Furthermore the essential standards for data management and data consistency agreed
upon in the AEC industry are presented. Furthermore the concepts and syntax of the
CCS system is covered.
2.1
Current practices
The BIM adoption in the AEC domain is increasing worldwide, but although the industry
have recognized the potentials of adopting BIM, the AEC domain is still being criticized
for the lack of efficiency, productivity, quality and sustainability.
Although BIM is
believed and acknowledged to have the ability to improve the points of critique, the
implementation of BIM is still facing challenges. The challenges are mainly consisting of
miscommunication primarily between the various domains but also the communication
between the used software tools (Arayici, Egbu, and Coates 2012). A domain-specific
expert uses a language and a terminology specific to the domain and in accordance
with the experts area of knowledge. Domain-specific BIM tools are developed to aid
the expert with several tasks within the domain and similarly uses a language and
terminology foreign for other domains. This causes a multiplication of various languages
and terminologies used in the current AEC industry. Therefore a great amount of work is
needed for sustainable collaboration and management of the diverse information between
project members.
2.1.1
BIM Process & Collaboration
BIM has mistakenly been understood as a synonym to 3D CAD drawings and virtual
building models solely. Although the proper understanding of BIM is gaining clarity in
the AEC industry, the full adoption of BIM requires more understanding of the BIM
process and the interaction of information between the domains throughout (NBS 2016)
(Waterhouse et al. 2015).
==PAGE:24==
6
2 BIM workflow and standards
Figure 2.1: The full process of BIM in a project from idea to operation/maintenance.
The different domains both contributes to and receive information from
the centralized common BIM model (Karlshøj 2013).
The BIM process requires a high level of communication and the participants in each
domain need specific standards for data exchanges in order to fully extract the poten-
tials of BIM. Many different protocols and guidelines have been developed to uniform
the information structure and thereby support the work flow in the project. Amongst
these is an approach developed by Molio (former Bips), where the information in the
different stages of the project is divided into different levels e.g. information levels (Gür-
tle, Baumgärtel, and Scherer 2015). The Level of Detail (LOD) is a similar approach
developed to control the amount of information in the BIM process. The chosen LOD
specifies the amount of information to be stored in the BIM model. Understanding the
type and amount of available information in a project cycle is a necessity to establish
an optimal BIM-based workflow.
The stage of the project life-cycle of such design-projects has an impact on the level
of the total information and the actual valid information, which is available at a specific
time of the project. Figure 2.2 shows the typical development of the total information
compared to the valid information throughout a building projectCuneco 2012b.
A common understanding of the tasks and information related to the BIM process
is therefore needed. This has yielded the development of the Information Delivery Man-
uals (IDM). In order to standardize the work of the different domains IDMs have been
==PAGE:25==
2.1 Current practices
7
time
Information
Information in 
project material
Valid information
in project material
Figure 2.2: Illustration of the development of the total amount of information in a
building project compared to the amount of valid information (Cuneco
2012b).
developed to specify the required in- and outgoing information for the different tasks.
Understanding the in- and outgoing information will support the development of more
precise Model View Definitions (MVD), which will reduce the challenge related to the
lack of interoperability between software tools in the AEC industry (BuildingSMART
2016d).
2.1.2
Interoperability
Various software tools have been developed to facilitate different tasks related to the
domains.
These software solutions have individual abilities to perform work-related
tasks and will generate information, which will be included in the information exchange
between the different domains. The information exchange between the different domain-
specific tools needs to be interoperable in order to establish a proper collaboration- and
work-flow. The major software vendors within the AEC industry such as Autodesk, Bent-
ley and Graphisoft have each developed operable software products within the native
file-formats. This has created a challenge to the interoperability between the different
BIM tools across the software vendors. In order to deal with these challenges some of
the software vendors are aiming to develop software tools able to cover a wider spec-
trum of BIM related tasks. For example has Autodesk Revit combined architectural-,
structural- and MEP-disciplines within the .rvt format in order to obtain interoper-
ability. This solution forces and limits the project stakeholders to use BIM tools from
a single software vendor. This has therefore generated another approach allowing for
data exchanges across software-platforms. buildingSMART has developed the Industry
Foundation Classes (IFC) as the open standard for the AEC domain (BuildingSMART
2016d). IFC allows for both software-vendors and end-users to benefit from a common
language for data exchange. In order to obtain this interoperability for the Danish sec-
tor, governmental efforts have been put into establishing rules and regulations to use the
==PAGE:26==
8
2 BIM workflow and standards
open standards such as IFC. The Danish Information and Communication Technology
(ICT) regulation explicitly prescribes the use of the IFC format for purposes related to
the representation of BIM models and exchanges of discipline models (Ministeriet for
By - Bolig og Landdistrikter 2013).
2.2
Industry Foundation Classes
builldingSMART aims to obtain semantic interoperability by developing and maintaining
the following standards shown on Figure 2.3, which also highlights the core concepts of
buildingSMART.
Figure 2.3: Illustration of buildingSMART’s standards defined in ISO. Original figure
in (BuildingSMART 2016a).
The International Framework for Dictionaries (IFD) is the multilingual terminology,
which is used to store and define information about construction works. Based on this
the buildingSMART Data Dictionary (bSDD) is developed, which aims to exchange
accurate names, terms and other relevant information across domains and languages.
As mentioned previously IDMs represent the definition of processes and which data is
exchanged. The IFC schema is a set of data representations and entity declarations
related to building information. Over 150 software applications supports import or/and
export of IFC data, which has made the IFC to the open standard for BIM data exchange
(BuildingSMART 2017b).
2.2.1
Technical overview
The IFC specify a data schema using the EXPRESS data definition language defined
in ISO10303-11.
The file formats for exchanging and sharing data according to the
conceptual schema is Clear text encoding of the exchange structure, defined in ISO 10303-
21.
ISO 10303 is the Standard for the Exchange of Product model (STEP). STEP
provides a neutral computer-interpretable representation of data independent of any
system. SCRA 2006
==PAGE:27==
2.2 Industry Foundation Classes
9
EXPRESS is a textual, conceptual and object-oriented schema language, where the
main elements are:
• Schema
• Type
• Entity
The schema is the container, where the contents are all the EXPRESS elements; types,
entities, rules, functions etc. The types are used to represent value domains and used to
define the attributes and properties of entities. They can consist of the simple built-in
types; REAL, INTEGER, STRING, BOOLEAN etc. Types can also be user-defined or of the
’enumeration types’ written as ENUMERATION OF(X,Y,Z) or the ’select types’ defined
as SELECT(X,Y,Z. The entities represents the objects and concepts of interest, such
as ENTITY Wall or ENTITY Door.
Other elements in the notation language, such as
rules and functions are also available. Using the SUBTYPE OF and SUPERTYPE OF allow a
hierarchical structure and more semantic approach to relationships between entities.
2.2.2
Structure of IFC
The complete data schema of IFC is divided and categorized into four conceptual layers,
each with individual schemas:
1. Resource layer
2. Core layer
3. Interoperability layer
4. Domain layer
The resource data schemas consists of supporting data structures, where entities cannot
exist independently, but can only exist if referenced by entities from the other layers.
Therefore these entities does not have a Global Unique Identifier (GUID) unless they
are referenced.
The schemas in the core layer provides the common concepts, basic
relationships and fundamental structure of the data models. The layer represents the
entity definitions, which can be referenced and specialized by entities in the upper layers.
The main schema in this layer is the IfcKernel, which defines the most abstract parts of
the specification. In this schema the IfcRoot entity is defined, which is the most abstract
entity.
The interoperability layer contains the shared elements schemas. Entities in this
layer are specific to resources, products or processes used across several domains. An
example of a data schema in this layers is ’IfcSharedBldgElements’ consisting of different
entities used across disciplines such as IfcDoor, IfcBeam, IfcWall etc.
The last layer is the domain layer, where the schemas and entities therein are related
to the specific domains. In contrast to the Interoperability layer, where the entities
==PAGE:28==
10
2 BIM workflow and standards
are utilized for inter-domain data exchange, the definitions in this layer are utilized
for intra-domain data exchange. Examples of the schemas related to this layer is the
’IfcHVACDomain’, where the entities IfcPump, IfcValve etc. are represented.
The architecture of the layers and the related IFC schemas are shown on Figure 2.4
Figure 2.4: Illustration of the structure of IFC. Original figure in (BuildingSMART
2016b).
The sum of these schemas represents the entire IFC specification. The latest released
version of the IFC specification, IFC4 - Addendum 2 contains 130 Defined types, 207
Enumeration types, 60 Select types and 774 Entities (BuildingSMART 2016b). buildings-
mart is constantly expanding the IFC specification. The predecessor IFC4 Addendum
1 had 768 Entities (BuildingSMART 2015) and its predecessor IFC4 had 766 entities.
(BuildingSMART 2013)
2.2.3
Model View Definition
The IFC of a BIM model is not a complete representation of the models information but
rather a subset defined by the used MVD. MVD is the subset of IFC suited for a partic-
ular workflow. MVDs allow for a lighter-weight IFC export with specific properties for a
given use. MVDs can be user-defined or offical following the buildingSMART guidelines.
==PAGE:29==
2.2 Industry Foundation Classes
11
buildingSMART has published several MVDs related to the IFC specifications. The
current official buildingSMART releases of the MVDs conforming to IFC4 are:
• Design Transfer View (IFC4DTV)
• Reference View (IFC4RV)
The MVDs convert the software-specific file to an IFC file. The representation of
the data conforming to used EXPRESS schema is following the ISO 10303-21 Clear text
encoding of exchange structure also called the p21-file (BuildingSMART 2017a). This
format is structured with an instance per line and consists of two main sections. A
HEADER section, containing the meta data of the file and a DATA section containing all
the information and data of the instances conforming to the used MVD. An example of
the p21 file is listed in Listing 2.1

The instance numbers in the data section are arbitrary and can change if the file is

As mentioned earlier there is a need of standards ad legislation for the practical
utilization of open formats and also to push the adoption of such methods.
2.3
Information and Communication
Technology
Besides the requirements concerning the use of the IFC format the Danish authorities
have also prescribed the use of the ICT agreements to manage and ensure uniform and
standardized information exchanges. The ICT agreement consists of 7 ICT specifications,
each covering a specific part of digital collaboration:
• A102-1 Classification
• A102-2 Digital Communication
• A102-3 Establishment of a Communication Platform
• A102-4 Digital Planning
• A102-5 Digital Tender
• A102-6 Quantities
• A102-7 Digital Delivery
The goal of the implementation of the ICT specifications is to harmonize and uniform
the use of information and communication technologies in the AEC domain and thereby
reduce miscommunications and increase the efficiency. The ICT regulation makes it
mandatory for the AEC industry to use classification systems in order to structure
the data and make the information coherent in the project material. In the first ICT
specification A102-1 - Classification three models can be followed (Bips 2016):
• Model A - the consultant decides which classification system to use.
• Model B - the classification system has to be Cuneco Classification System (CCS).
• Model P - the client decides which classification system to use.
==PAGE:31==
2.4 Building Classification systems
13
The models A and P gives more freedom to the consultant and the client respectively.
If model B is chosen in the ICT agreement, the classification has to be CCS (Bips 2016).
2.4
Building Classification systems
Various efforts have been put in to developing systems in order to classify components
and objects in the AEC projects. MT Højgaard published a white paper in June 2015,
where a survey was conducted with 197 domain specific BIM models (Højgaard 2015).
Here it was found, that the commonly used classification systems in the Danish AEC
industry covers ’Samarbetskomitén för Bygg nads- frågor’ (SfB), CCS, BIM7aa, ’For-
valtningsklassifikation’ (FK), ’Dansk Byggeklassifikation’ (DBK) amongst others. The
survey concluded, that 48% of the BIM models have been using SfB for classification,
where DBK, CCS and BIM7aa altogether represents 11% of the BIMs classification
codes. The survey also stresses on the fact, that the ICT regulations are driving the
use of classification in the Danish AEC industry. Here it is experienced, that the use of
classification is considerably increased when governed by the Danish ICT regulations.
2.4.1
CCS
Molio has developed the Cuneco Classification System (CCS) in order to structure and
uniform the representation of information in AEC projects. The purpose of the devel-
opment of CCS is to provide a structured coding system to ensure a uniform and clear
information understanding between the stakeholders and support information exchanges.
CCS gives a clear identity to each part of the building and can be used on every project
phase from project start to maintenance (Cuneco 2012a)
Furthermore is CCS believed to replace mainly DBK, but also SfB in the Danish AEC
domain. The approach CCS is using is different from the use of traditional classification
systems, where the classification code of the same entity changes during the project-cycle
by the adding of properties, attributes etc. CCS maintains the same classification code
with relevant information attached as the project develops (Cuneco 2012a). Furthermore
are the types of information about different objects in a building divided into several
categories in CCS. Amongst these are the ’Classification’, ’Identification’ and ’Properties’.
Figure 2.5 shows the remaining parts, which CCS deals with.
In the following only the classification and identification areas will be focused upon.
The purpose of the classification is to distinguish between different categories of objects,
where the identification defines the objects within the same category at an occurence-
level. Summarizing these points it is understood that CCS classifies object types and
identifies object occurences.
CCS operates with a structure of systems with hierarchical composition within dif-
ferent CCS domains.
The domains are assigned with a topnode in square brackets,
which represent classes of objects within the domain. Table 2.1 shows some examples of
domains with related topnodes.
==PAGE:32==

2.4.3
Identification
The CCS identification codes are dependent on the classification codes, since these are
used to create the identification codes. The identification codes are used to distinguish
between instances/occurances e.g. window no. 1 and window no. 2, introducing an ID
number to the code. The following list shows the seven types of identification codes
included in this project thesis:
• CCS single-level ID
• CCS type-ID
• CCS multi-level ID
• CCS multi-level type-ID
• CCS single-level location-at ID
• CCS functional-ID

2.4.4
CCS software tools
Several tools are being developed to support CCS in common BIM software. Classify
focuses on the assignment of CCS -, FK - and SfB classification codes in Autodesk Revit
models. Classify support the following in CCS classification and identification:
==PAGE:37==
2.5 Sub-conclusion
19
• CCSClassCode
• CCSTypeID
• CCSClassName
• CCSSingleLevelID
It is also possible to assign different aspects of the classification codes related to the SfB
and FK systems. Another developed tool is the Standard Project Information Network
Exchange (Spine), a Revit plug-in, where information about building components can
be classified with regards to CCS classification and identification codes. Spine supports
a wider scope than Classify and it is possible to perform consistency checks and to assign
properties to the classification and identification codes. Spine also supports mapping
between the classification systems FK, SfB and BIM7aa. Both for Classify and Spine
the process of assigning the classification codes is partly manual (Spine 2017b) (NTI
2017).
2.5
Sub-conclusion
The AEC domain is undertaking a development in the adoption and understanding of
BIM. With the BIM adoption in AEC projects a great amount of diverse information is
generated. This demands a great effort put in management and collaboration of not only
project members, but also of the used BIM software. Due to the lack of interoperability
between the different BIM tools open standards as IFC are developed. IFC uses the EX-
PRESS notation language, where a BIM model can be represented intelligently in IFC
compatible software. The IFC format is prescribed to use in the Danish ICT regulation
along with classification systems to structure the diverse information in a coherent man-
ner. The ICT specifications stresses on the utilization of CCS as classification system.
The systematic structure of CCS differs from traditional classification systems as the
system is divided into multiple aspects. Within the classification different objects can
be classified differently depending on the hierarchy. Similarly with the identification,
where the codes can have six different structures depending on the type of identification
code. This seems to generate a great amount of information about building components,
which Molio claims to be uniform and easy to interpret both by humans and software.
However the different amount of codes and the related information can be confusing and
may cause room for human-related mistakes in the work with CCS. Software products
such as Spine and Classify have developed tools to semi-automatically assign different
classification - and identification codes in Autodesk Revit projects. This type of solution
may be seen as an optimization of the domain’s work with classification systems, never-
theless these solutions are still limited. Firstly both Spine and Classify are supporting
Autodesk Revit only, which reduces the scope of domains not using Autodesk Revit.
Secondary Spine and Classify users are still obliged to manually assign classification and
==PAGE:38==
20
2 BIM workflow and standards
identification codes. Tertiary the tools are not yet supporting the aspects of CCS to a
full extent. Therefore there is still room for improvements in the work with CCS.
==PAGE:39==
CHAPTER 3
Linked Data and The
Semantic Web
This chapter represents the second part of the literature study. In this part of the thesis
the principles, terminology and technologies of the Semantic Web and Linked data is
presented. The majority of the examples given in the following sections are produced
by the authors.
3.1
Principles and concepts
The Semantic Web is a term denoted to represent The World Wide Web Consortium’s
(W3C) concept of a linked web of data (W3C 2017). It is an extension or successor to the
World Wide Web in which the majority of web pages and web data are in a distributed
network, represented in a human-readable format, predominately the HyperText Markup
Language (HTML), and interrelated using Uniform Resource Locators (URLs) (Doan
et al. 2002), (Niknam and Karshenas 2015). The information stored in these document-
based representations of webpages cannot be interpreted or processed by computers,
since the current formats of the web doesn’t have any syntax, which understands the
semantics of data (Berners-Lee 2006). Hence a new data format is needed to realize the
full potential of the extensive amount of data available on the web. This format should
allow for computers-agents to access collections of information, manipulate and reason
the semantics, and also infer new relationships and links and thereby creating a linked
network of date (Berners-Lee, Hendler, and Lassila 2001). As mentioned in Berners-Lee,
Hendler, and Lassila (2001):
“The Semantic Web will enable machines to comprehend semantic documents and
data, not human speech and writings.”
Standardizing data on the web requires a format, which is able to represent multiple
knowledge domains without limitations such as ambiguity as is often present in human
speech and writing. Furthermore it should have a strong logical basis in order to express
complex properties and hierarchies of data, but also to reason links, similarities and
differences of data information (Berners-Lee, Hendler, and Lassila 2001).
W3C has
introduced a number of technologies and conceptual rules to realize the goals of the
semantic web. In the following sections these technologies and concepts will be presented.
==PAGE:40==
22
3 Linked Data and The Semantic Web
In the following section the data format and identification protocol of The Semantic Web
are outlined.
3.2
Data format and identifiers
3.2.1
Resource Description Framework
The basis of representing data in the Semantic Web lies in the representation of data in a
directed labelled graph-based data model (Pauwels and Terkaj 2016). To accommodate
this knowledge representation a data format called Resource Description Framework
(RDF) is utilized, which represents data in so-called ’RDF triples’ (Berners-Lee, Hendler,
and Lassila 2001). This specific syntax constructs information as a graph-based data
model by a triple structure consisting of a subject, a predicate and an object as illustrated
in Figure 3.1 (W3C 2014a). The three elements of a triple denotes something of the
universal knowledge and they are collectively specified as resources (W3C 2014a).
Object
Predicate
Subject
Figure 3.1: Anatomy of a RDF triple.
Each node i.e. the subject and object represents some concept or idea of any knowl-
edge domain (Pauwels and Terkaj 2016). The predicate binds the two nodes and there-
fore describes the property or relationship between the subject and object. Hence this
uniform representation of information presents a direction similar to a logical statement,
and these triples are also known as RDF Statements. RDF graphs are conjunctions (log-
ical AND) of their triples (W3C 2014a). For instance the following statement ”Beam
A is bolted to Beam B” can easily be represented in a triple format, since the subject
is ’Beam A’, the predicate is ’bolted to’ and the object is ’Beam B’. Figure 3.2 illus-
trates this in a sentence and graph representation. Hence by representing information
in a RDF syntax computers would comprehend that certain concepts, objects, things
have properties connecting them with other concepts or even values - creating a web of
interlinked data (Berners-Lee, Hendler, and Lassila 2001).
"Beam A is bolted to Beam B"
Beam B
bolted to
Beam A
Figure 3.2: Correlation of statements and triples.
==PAGE:41==
3.2 Data format and identifiers
23
3.2.2
Universal Resource Identifiers
Since multiple instances on the web or in any document, sheet or application could
be named ’Beam A’ an identifying protocol is required, so that information can be
distinguished and identified. Similar to web pages being identified by their Uniform
Resource Locators (URLs) the subject, predicate and object of each triples are given a
unique identifier by a Universal Resource Identifier (URI) of which URLs are a common
type (Berners-Lee, Hendler, and Lassila 2001). The nodes in a triple are of three kinds:
Internationalized Resource Identifiers (IRIs), literals, and blank nodes, which are all
generally known as RDF Terms (W3C 2014a). IRIs are a generalization of URIs, but
allows for usage of a wider range of Unicode characters (W3C 2014a). In the remainder
of this thesis the term URI and IRI will be used interchangeably. URIs and literals
identifies resources, but their function of expression differs. URIs generally identifies
unique resources such as building elements, a theory or a country, and these are named
referents. Literals denotes resources of data types known as literal values, such as strings,
dates, numbers, integers etc. Blank nodes are individuals without explicit names, but
with some specific attributes (W3C 2014a). These nodes are disjoint from URIs and
literals and a further elaboration on this concept will be given later in this paper.
Resources are defined as URIs by some namespace in a hyperlink http format sim-
ilar to the hyperlinks known from the web e.g. http://www.google.com. Hence any
referent has an unique namespace URI, whereby the resource is dereferencable by a
computer-agent i.e. it is accessible for a remote server (W3C 2014a). While writing
RDF graphs these namespaces can be abbreviated for readability purposes by imple-
menting a namespace prefix. The prefix is not restricted and could be any valid string.
The syntax for writing the namespaces and prefix depends on the format of the RDF
graph.
A textual representation of RDF graphs can be done in several serialization
formats, such as RDF/XML (.rdf), Turtle (.ttl), N-triples (.n_t) or Notation-3 (.n3)
(Pauwels and Terkaj 2016) (W3C 2014a).
To exemplify the concept of namespace
URIs and namespace prefixes Table 3.1 shows a representation in Turtle notation for
the triple from Figure 3.2. The abbreviated URI is therefore written as prefix:suffix,
where for instance the namespace prefix is thesis: and the chosen suffix represent-
ing ’Beam A’ is BeamA. Thus the namespace prefix thesis: substitutes the full URI
http://www.thesis.org/examples/#, hence allowing for simpler writing and reading
of RDF triples.
Literal value resources are mapped to a datatype URI by the use of The XML Schema
Built-in Datatypes created by W3C (W3C 2014a). The namespace URI for this schema
is http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema# and a common prefix is xsd:. A selection of
the datatypes included in the XML Schema are listed in Table 3.2.
Blank nodes are as mentioned disjoint from URIs and literals, since they only denote
the existence of an individual with some attributes. These nodes don’t have an identifier
in a RDF syntax, but are locally identified in the file or RDF graph in which they are
defined (W3C 2014a). Hence blank node identifiers have dependencies to the specific
syntax chosen for the definition of a given triple set or RDF graph. Blank nodes can be
used for resources that don’t require a name or for representing multivalued relationships
==PAGE:42==
24

3.2.3
RDF serializations
As previously stated about the different syntaxes for writing RDF called serializations,
the formats Turtle and N-triples were introduced. Turtle is one of the commonly used
syntaxes for writing RDF graphs allowing for writing triples with abbreviations in a
textually compact form (Beckett et al. 2014).
N-triples are a subset of Turtle and
follows a line-based text format for encoding RDF graphs (Beckett 2014). Listing 3.1
shows an example of defining the statements ’Wall A has an opening X’, ’Opening X is
filled by Window Y’, and ’Window Y has width 4000 mm’ in the N-triples and Turtle
syntax. In N-triples the subject, predicate, and object and their respective URIs are
written in brackets <>, and a triple is ended with a punctuation. In Turtle prefixes are
supported, which are the initial part of any Turtle RDF graph. Another useful feature
is the @base which defines the namespace URI native to the particular default graph,
thus the namespace prefix can be omitted and substituted with a colon :. In Listing
3.1 however the @base has not been included in order to illustrate the usage of prefix.
Throughout this paper any example concept presented as RDF graphs are represented by
==PAGE:43==
3.2 Data format and identifiers
25
the prefix thesis: with namespace URI http://www.thesis.org/examples/#. Turtle
triples also ends with a punctuation to determine that a given triple ends. Since many
triples can define the same subject or object value symbols semicolons ’;’ and commas’,’
are used. The semicolon ’;’ is for the repetition of triples with the same subject, which
only varies in predicate and object (Beckett et al. 2014). In Listing 3.1 this is shown for
the Turtle syntax, where for ’Window Y’ the height and the storey location are added.
Furthermore repetition of objects are also possible, for instance while describing the
name of a given subject in multiple languages, and the syntax used for this operation
is the comma ’,’. This is shown for the subject thesis:wall_A, where the type name
is described in English and Danish. Here the language tag @ is introduced by which it
is possible to define the language of a given string (Beckett et al. 2014). Also it should
be noted that in Turtle it is not necessary to specify if a given literal value is a string,
hence "wall" is understood by Turtle to be a xsd:string.

3.2.4
Restrictions and RDF datasets
Since information on the web or in any universal knowledge domain are prone to changes
over time, some general restrictions must be placed within the RDF syntax. In the
recommendation RDF 1.1 Concepts and Abstract Syntax (W3C 2014a) some reflections
on changes to data and updating of knowledge within the context of RDF are stated:
==PAGE:44==
26
3 Linked Data and The Semantic Web
• An URI should not change its intended referent.
• Literals should remain constant and not change their value.
• Relationships between resources at one time may not hold at another time.
• A container of RDF graphs that may have a state that can change over time are
called RDF sources. Hence RDF sources may provide different RDF graphs at
different time.
• RDF sources may be constant snapshots of other RDF sources, thereby achieving
its current state at some point.
In practice RDF graphs are collected forming a RDF dataset in which the content
of the graphs are kept seperate. A RDF dataset comprises of exactly one default graph,
which doesn’t have a name and is possibly empty (W3C 2014a). Other graphs in the
dataset are called named graphs, which are pairs of URIs or blanks nodes with a RDF
graph. Within RDF graphs the terms entailment, equivalence, and inconsistency defines
the relationships between RDF graphs (W3C 2014a). A graph A entails another graph B
if every definition making graph A true also makes graph B true. Equivalence between
two graphs means that both graphs entail each other i.e.
the same definitions are
represented in both graphs. Inconsistency within a RDF graph is true, when an internal
contradiction is contained i.e. there are no possible arrangement of the world that makes
a definition in the graph true (W3C 2014a).
3.2.5
Limitations of stand-alone RDF syntax
Throughout this section so far the structure of RDF triples, the syntax of writing triples,
and the identification procedure of URIs for resources, literals, and blank nodes have
been outlined. One of the key goals of The Semantic Web is to enable machine-learning
and comprehension of the semantics of data information. Simply having a syntax for
writing statements in triples and identifying them by URIs doesn’t solve this problem.
Multiple databases on the web could specify the URI of the same concept in different
ways. Therefore a computer-agent wouldn’t ’understand’ i.e. deduce for instance that
indeed the object ’Beam A’ in one triple in database X describes the same concept (i.e.
beam is a structural building element) as the subject ’Beam B’ in database Y. Another
problem would be to map equivalence between concepts, terms etc. A ’Building Storey’
and ’Storey Level’ could be argued to define the same concept or for instance that
a ’Beam’ is a undercategory of ’Building Element’.
Hence both class hierarchy and
equivalence of data are of importance for enabling machine-learning and manipulation
(Berners-Lee, Hendler, and Lassila 2001). Machines need to be fed some information that
describes RDF resources in a descriptive, logical and unambiguous way, hence improving
the semantic structure of RDF graphs (Pauwels et al. 2011). In the following section
the technologies for enabling semantic comprehension of data is presented.
==PAGE:45==
3.3 Knowledge vocabularies and ontologies
27
3.3
Knowledge vocabularies and ontologies
To enrich the semantic expressivity of RDF graphs W3C has developed vocabularies and
ontologies supported by RDF. These are used to enable further extension and description
of RDF resources so that machines understand the semantics of both class and property
hierarchy, relationships and cardinality restrictions. Hence ambiguities between data
from different RDF graphs can be mitigated and thus both the consistency of data is
improved, but also the data integration (W3C 2015). The two most important vocabu-
laries, created by W3C for these purposes, are the RDF Schema and the Web Ontology
Language (DuCharme 2014).
3.3.1
TBox, ABox and RBox
In understanding the concept of named and default graphs it is important to note the
terms TBox, ABox and RBox. The TBox denotes the Terminological Box and represents
the domain ontology constituting of axioms being the class and property definitions also
referred collectively to as the schema (Zhang and Beetz 2015).
These axioms are a
representation of a domain of interest consisting of a terminology of statements with
descriptive and fixed meanings (W3C 2012b). The Assertional Box or ABox defines
the assertional knowledge being data instances semantically described via the schema
(TBox) (Sack 2016c). Finally RBox is a denotion for rulesets allowing for extended
reasoning and inferencing over the RDF graphs storing information of the TBox and
ABox (Pauwels et al. 2016). From the examples so far in this paper only assertion data
i.e. the ABox data has been introduced, such as the instance thesis:Wall_A. In order
to define the domain ontology i.e. the TBox, vocabularies and ontologies are needed.
Solutions and standards created for this purpose are presented next.
3.3.2
RDF Schema
RDF Schema (RDFS) extends the RDF vocabulary by providing a data-modelling
vocabulary in which class and properties hierarchies can be specified (W3C 2014b).
Furthermore labels and comments, list structures, as well as containers and collec-
tions can be specified with RDFS. In this specification the prefix rdfs: represents the
namespace URI http://www.w3.org/2000/01/rdf-schema#. Also RDFS uses http:
//www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#, which is the namespace for RDF with
prefix rdf:.
3.3.2.1
RDFS classes and properties
In RDFS rdfs:Resource is the class of all resources, hence every single class is a subclass
of rdfs:Resource (W3C 2014b). rdfs:Resource is an instance of rdfs:Class and
rdfs:Class is an instance of itself. Other classes defined in RDFS are thus instances of
==PAGE:46==
28
3 Linked Data and The Semantic Web
rdfs:Class and subclasses of rdfs:Resource. In Table 3.3 the key classes represented
in RDFS are listed.
Class name
Description
rdfs:Resource
Class of everything
rdfs:Class
Class of RDF classes
rdf:Property
Class of RDF properties
rdfs:Datatype
Class of RDF datatypes
rdfs:Literal
Class of literal values
rdfs:Container
Class of RDF containers
rdfs:List
Class of RDF lists
Table 3.3: Selection of classes in the RDF Schema (W3C 2014b).
To describe which class a instance belongs to and furthermore the hierarchy and
relations of classes, RDFS includes properties. One of the commonly used properties is
rdf:type, which defines that a given resource is an instance of a class (W3C 2014b). Due
to its key role in TBox definition an acronym a can be used instead of rdf:type. Two
key mechanisms in RDFS property definitions are rdfs:domain and rdfs:range, which
are instances of rdf:Property. The rdfs:domain is used to define that an instance
i.e. a resource of a given property belongs to a certain class or classes. In contrast
rdfs:range defines the value of a property to be an instance of one or more classes.
The property rdfs:subPropertyOf has both a rdfs:domain and rdfs:range that is
defined to be a rdf:Property. This makes sense when observing the following Listing
3.2. An illustration of Listing 3.2 is shown in Figure 3.3.

5 @prefix thesis: <http://www.thesis.org/examples/#> .

In the above rdfs:subClassOf enables the class hierarchy relations. With rdfs:
subClassOf the relationship defined is recursive meaning that the range is the so-called
super-class. The same principle is valid for rdfs:subPropertyOf, by which the super-
property can be inferred. The domain and range values for each property class is an
indicator for the expected application of the property and what the subject and object
values are restricted to. In the case of Listing 3.3 the thesis:haswidth property expects
an instance of class thesis:buildingelement as the subject and a literal value of
format xsd:integer as object value. However in the case of the property class thesis:
==PAGE:50==
32
3 Linked Data and The Semantic Web
isfilledby the expected object value is an instance of class thesis:fillingelement,
but their is not any triple matching this by definition.
However since the instance
thesis:window_B is of class thesis:windowelement and this class is a subclass of
thesis:fillingelement the range value is correct and understood.
Although even
without this subclass definition the range value would have inferred this relation by
reasoning, which expresses the power of the domain and range as also seen by the
previous examples. RDFS also comprises of properties that extend their definition or
relation to other sources. The rdfs:seeAlso and rdfs:isDefinedBy both points to
other resources, which either explains the subject or defines it.
3.3.2.3
List structures
The RDF Schema also has a syntax for defining list structures. These comprises of two
kinds, containers and collections (W3C 2014b). The difference between rdfs:Container
and rdfs:Collection are that containers are open i.e. can have more members, while
collections are closed and cannot be extended. Thus rdfs:Container is used for lists,
where extension of members make sense and should be possible. Instances or rdfs:
member of a rdfs:Container are written rdf_1, rdf_2, rdf_3 ... and specified by the
class rdfs:ContainerMembershipProperty, which is a subclass of rdf:Property (W3C
2014b). rdfs:Container is a super-class of rdf:Bag, rdf:Seq and rdf:Alt. While
similar these three subclasses of rdfs:Container intends to define different attributes
for the container:
• rdf:Bag marks that the container is unordered.
• rdf:Seq denotes that the numerical order of the rdfs:member are of significance.
• rdf:Alt is used to indicate that it is intended to choose one of the members of
the container during processing.
A rdfs:Collection has a list-like structure defined by rdfs:List. The first item
in a rdfs:List is defined by the property rdfs:first. The rest of the list or the tail
is defined by rdf:rest. Empty lists are defined by rdfs:nil. In writing list-structures
of either rdfs:Container or rdfs:Collection blank nodes are commonly used. In the
following a textual and graphic representation of an instance of rdfs:Container and
rdfs:Collection are presented to highlight the usage of blank nodes. The instance
thesis:ventilationplant_A has several members and is defined as a rdfs:Container
with subclass rdf:Bag, and also shown as rdfs:Collection. This example is shown in
Listing 3.4 and graphically in Figure 3.5.

<http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax -ns#> .
4 @prefix rdfs:
<http://www.w3.org/2000/01/rdf-schema#> .
5 @prefix thesis: <http://www.thesis.org/examples/#> .
6 @prefix xsd:
<http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema#> .

Despite the obvious increase of semantics of RDF graphs using RDFS there are
still limitations to this vocabulary. Working with open knowledge information elements
such as disjunction, likeness and equality of data is vital for consistency and richness
of data semantics. RDFS does not include this semantic richness by which for instance
equivalence or disjointness of resources can be expressed. In order to represent more
==PAGE:52==
34
3 Linked Data and The Semantic Web
expressive RDF statements and relations the Web Ontology Language (OWL) is intro-
duced by W3C (Pauwels and Terkaj 2016).
3.3.3
Web Ontology Language
The Web Ontology Language (OWL) is a semantic language for expressing ontologies
and the current specification developed by W3C is named OWL2 (W3C 2012b). Hence
further reference to OWL in this paper refers to OWL2. It functions as a computational
logic-based language and defines it semantics utilizing Description Logic and by exten-
sion the description logic SROIQ(D) (W3C 2012a). This induces some restrictions to
the full expressivity of OWL2, which is required if complying to the conditions of the
SROIQ(D) logic is needed (Pauwels and Terkaj 2016). When these conditions are met
OWL2 and the underlying expressivity is said to be OWL2 DL. Furthermore OWL2
is declarative in its description of state of matters within a certain domain of interest.
With its foundation in description logic reasoning, inferencing of implicit knowledge and
relations is possible with this knowledge representation language.
3.3.3.1
OWL profiles
OWL2 and thus OWL2 DL has a subset of profiles by which the level of expressivity
is limited compared to the full OWL2 ontology and thereby the effeciency of querying
and reasoning through RDF graphs can be increased (Pauwels and Terkaj 2016). The
key profiles of OWL2 are OWL2 EL, OWL2 RL and OWL2 QL and their relation to
OWL2 is illustrated in Figure 3.6 (W3C 2012c). As mentioned ontologies and vocabu-
laries provides constructs for reasoning and inference of data. The need of profiles are
directly linked to this concept, since by limiting the constructs available in each profile
increase the performance of engines responsible for reasoning and inference. Hence when
specifying the TBox through OWL it is important to reflect on these profiles according
to the level of performance needed. In short the three profiles can be summarised as
follows (W3C 2012c) (Pauwels and Terkaj 2016):
• OWL2 RL: is aimed at applications that trades the full expressivity of the OWL
language for efficient and scalable reasoning.
• OWL2 QL: is for applications using large numbers of instance data requiring
efficient performance of query answering.
• OWL2 EL: is aimed at applications with ontologies containing large numbers of
properties and/or classes. Basic reasoning is available with the time of querying
being polynomial with respect to the size of ontology.
==PAGE:53==
3.3 Knowledge vocabularies and ontologies
35
OWL2 Full 
RL
QL
EL
DL
Figure 3.6: Venn Diagram of OWL2 profiles. Original figure in (W3C 2009).
3.3.3.2
Open- and close-world assumption
In knowledge base creation and especially the use of ontologies to represent information
applicable in a Semantic Web and Linked Data context, it is important to understand
and distinguish between the Open-World Assumption (OWA) and the Close-World As-
sumption (CWA) (Sack 2016b). The CWA is normally the default assumption in tradi-
tional software applications, such as Autodesk Revit or AutoCAD. Here anything that is
not specified must be considered by the application as false and hence in broader terms
as non-existing or untrue. In contrast incomplete information is common on the web and
generally in any knowledge domain and this is also the default position in Semantic Web
technologies covered by the OWA. In the OWA unspecified statements or information
are neither false or true, but regarded as unknown (Pauwels and Terkaj 2016). The
strategy behind applying the OWA is the intentional underspecification of knowledge
bases, whereby it is expected that these models are extended and reused by others (Sack
2016b). Thus something unspecified is not necessarily false or not true, but could at
the given moment be unknown, while it may become true by further specification in the
future.
3.3.3.3
OWL semantics
OWL further enhances the semantics of working with RDF statements by the use of
description logic. Thus negation, disjunction and other restrictions can be implemented
in the definition of classes and properties and their relations and hierarchies. The names-
pace URI for OWL is http://www.w3.org/2002/07/owl# with the common namespace
prefix of owl:. As with RDFS there exists a Turtle notation for OWL (W3C 2012b).
The hierarchy of building blocks and semantics in OWL are illustrated in Figure 3.7 and
the concepts are presented in the following.

3.3.3.4
Class definitions
The basic building blocks of OWL consists of individuals to represents objects, classes to
define categories, and properties to define relations similar to that of RDFS. These build-
ing blocks are utilized to represent the statements or axioms of the ontology. OWL in-
cludes RDFS as a subset to define axioms. More specifically the rdf:type, rdfs:domain,
rdfs:range, rdfs:subClassOf and rdfs:subPropertyOf are necessary building blocks
for OWL definitions.
The basic predefined classes in OWL are owl:Thing and owl:Nothing. Similar to
rdfs:Resource the class definition owl:Thing is the super-class for all individuals, while
owl:Nothing is an empty class. Individuals are comparable with instances in RDFS
and are commonly defined by either a class-membership or without class-membership
as named individuals (Sack 2016c). Class-membership is stated by using the property
rdf:type as predicate with the object value of owl:Class. This also the syntax for defin-
ing classes in OWL. Individuals defined without class-membership are defined similarly
although the object value is set to the OWL class owl:NamedIndividual. In contrast
to RDFS, the OWL ontology allows for defining equivalence of resources, which is espe-
cially important when the objective is to interlink sources of information from different
knowledge domains. Class hierarchies is covered by the rdfs:subClassOf, but OWL in-
troduces owl:equivalentClass to define semantically equivalent classes. As described
in previous sections equivalence between classes requires that both classes contain the
exact same individuals (W3C 2012b).
Equivalence of individuals is also possible to
state using OWL by the use of the property class owl:SameAs.
Another important
characteristic of resources is disjointness, which can be defined between two classes with
owl:disjointWith or of several disjoint classes by owl:AllDisjointClasses. Similarly
==PAGE:55==
3.3 Knowledge vocabularies and ontologies
37
disjunctiveness of individuals can be defined for a pair or for multiple individuals by the
use of owl:differentFrom and owl:AllDifferent respectively.
Figure 3.8 and 3.9
shows graph-based representations of the concepts of equivalence and disjunctiveness.

3.3.3.5
Complex class representations
Further complex representation of classes and individuals is possible through logical class
constructors (W3C 2012b). These consists of the owl:intersectionOf, owl:unionOf
and owl:complementOf. The owl:intersectionOf is the conjunction, which allows
for expressing that a given class or individual results from the exact individuals, which
are instances of both classes being intersected (Sack 2016c) (W3C 2012a).
The use
of owl:unionOf has basis in logical disjunction, whereby the union of multiple classes
states that the individual is contained in at least one of the classes. Logical negation is
possible through owl:complementOf, which refers to exactly those objects, which are not
members of the class of the subject. In order to quantify the understanding and usage
of these logical class constructors the following Listing 3.5 exemplifies these concepts.
==PAGE:56==

3.3.3.6
Property definitions and restrictions
OWL allows for further semantic expression of property classes and hence the rdf:
Property class.
Two additional variants are introduced in OWL namely the owl:
ObjectProperty and owl:DatatypeProperty. As their name suggest these property
classes have rdfs:range values of classes and datatypes respectively. Complex classes
can be defined by property restrictions on values and cardinality.
For both restric-
tion patterns the constructors owl:Restriction and owl:onProperty are necessary
to include.
The operators for the definition of property restrictions on values are
owl:hasValue, owl:allValuesFrom and owl:someValuesFrom. The function of these
operators is to define a class of individuals by restricting the range value of a reasonable
property. These definitions can be nested and the following Listing 3.6 shows examples
of property restrictions.

Cardinality restrictions allows for defining classes containing individuals by restrict-
ing the range value of a property by an exact number or within ranges of numbers.
The constructors for these operations are owl:cardinality for exact number restric-
tion, and owl:minCardinality and owl:maxCardinality for defining lower and upper
bounds. It is also possible to assign these number restrictions to a certain class by the
use of the operators owl:qualifiedCardinality, owl:minQualifiedCardinality and
owl:maxQualifiedCardinality (Sack 2016d).
3.3.3.7
Property logics and chains
Relationships and characteristics of properties can be defined in OWL. A property
thesis:fillsopening can be defined as an owl:inverseOf another property thesis:
openingfilledby. Equivalence, disjointness and hierarchies of properties has a simi-
lar syntax in OWL as for classes and inviduals. Following the First-Order Logic, re-
lationships of properties can be stated in OWL. A property class X is said to be a
owl:FunctionalProperty if it holds true that if a subject A is related to object B by
X, and subject A is related to object C by X, then B and C must be equal.
Relationships between classes or individuals can be inferred on the basis of property
chain axioms referred to in OWL as owl:propertyChainAxiom. An example of applying
this concept could be to infer that a window placed in a wall is contained by the wall.
In First-Order Logic notation this would be written as:
∀w,o,v : hasopening(w,o) ∧isfilledby(o,v) →containselement(w,v)
, where w=Wall, o=Opening and v=Window.
An illustration of this concept is given in Figure 3.10. Hence by matching instance
data with the chain of properties new relationships are inferred in an intuitive manner.
Creating RDF datasets of knowledge domains leads to many triples as seen from the
examples given so far. An intelligent way of querying these triples needs to be present in
order for users to subtract subsets of information available in the RDF graph by a given
set of conditions. Furthermore remote RDF datasets needs to be accessed by which
==PAGE:58==
40
3 Linked Data and The Semantic Web
other end-users can query diverse data sources via the web. In the following the query
protocol utilized in Semantic Web technologies will be presented.
thesis:wall
thesis:opening
thesis:hasopening
thesis:isfilledby
thesis:containselement
thesis:window
Figure 3.10: Inference of relationship by introduction of property chain axiom.
3.4
SPARQL Protocol and RDF Query
Language
SPARQL acronym for ’SPARQL Protocol and RDF Query Language’ is the query lan-
guage developed for the Semantic Web (W3C 2013b). Together with the data format
RDF and vocabulary syntaxes RDFS and OWL, it represents the basic building blocks
of creating and using Linked Data. SPARQL allows for querying RDF graphs and ex-
ploring both explicit and implicit data from both local and external sources using the
HTTP protocol. The following sections will describe the structure of SPARQL queries,
the supported functions and how remote data is accessed and queried with SPARQL.
3.4.1
Structure
The format of writing SPARQL queries is similar to writing RDF graphs. The prefix ab-
breviation of namespaces is supported and queries are written using the triple notation
together with some query-language specific constructors (W3C 2013b). This triple no-
tation is the RDF Turtle serialization, which has been used throughout this paper. The
basic building blocks of a SPARQL query can be summarized in five elements: prefix dec-
larations, dataset definitions, query type and variable definitions, query graph pattern,
and modifiers (Feigenbaum and Prud’hommeaux 2013). The dataset definitions and
modiers are optional constructors and SPARQL queries are executable without these.
In Figure 3.11 the syntax for writing SPARQL are illustrated.
A SPARQL query begins with prefix declarations similar to the syntax used in RDF
graph creation. Within any RDF dataset a multitude of named graphs can be stored
and the FROM clause defines which dataset to query. SPARQL query defines variables
that match a query graph pattern. These variables are initially unknowns and are placed
strategically as substitutes of the subject, predicate, object resources from triples in the
RDF graph. Hence when SPARQL queries the RDF graph and the query graph pattern
==PAGE:59==
3.4 SPARQL Protocol and RDF Query Language
41
# prefix declarations
PREFIX thesis: <http://www.thesis.org/examples/#>
...
# dataset definitions
FROM ?graph
# query type and variable definitions
SELECT ?a ?b ?c
# query graph pattern
WHERE  {
...
}
# modifiers
LIMIT ...
Figure 3.11: Structure of a SPARQL query. Inspired by original figure in (Feigenbaum
and Prud’hommeaux 2013).
matches a subset of the dataset, then the solutions to the variables are outputted, which
are also called variable bindings. A binding is thus a pair of a variable with an RDF
term (W3C 2013b). The SELECT and WHERE constructors are the essential builders of
any SPARQL query. In the WHERE clause the graph pattern is defined including some
variables. SELECT states which of the variable bindings is to be outputted (DuCharme
2014).
Hence multiple variables can exist in the query graph pattern, but only the
variables specified by SELECT are outputted. Other query types exists in SPARQL, such
as DESCRIBE, ASK and CONSTRUCT (Sack 2016f). DESCRIBE outputs data of the variable
bindings, ASK answers with boolean operators if the graph pattern has bindings, and
CONSTRUCT creates user-defined RDF triple data if variable bindings exists (Sack 2016f).
For readability purposes the prefix definitions are omitted in the examples given of
SPARQL queries in this paper.
Listing 3.7 shows a basic SPARQL query of the RDF triple data from Listing 3.3.
Both TBox and ABox can be specified to be included in the dataset, whereby SPARQL
will query over a triples from both boxes. Listing 3.7 should query the dataset and
match the variable ?element with any subject resource that is of rdf:type thesis:
wallelement in the dataset. In Table 3.4 the results of the query are highlighted. In this
case only one resource was bounded by the variable ?element namely thesis:wall_A.
1 SELECT ?element
2 WHERE {
3
?element a thesis:wallelement.
4 }

Multiple query matches can occur and the bindings of many variables can be out-
putted at once. In general all the supported formats of defining triples, such as literals,
numeric datatypes etc. are also supported in SPARQL. In Listing 3.8 an example of
a query pattern including multiple variables are shown. First the query binds subjects
that are defined as a property class to the variable ?property. The next triple uses the
bindings found for ?property and finds any subject instance that has a width of ”1200”
according to the dataset. For those instances the class is found and finally the respective
super-class is found and bounded to the variable ?superclass. In SPARQL there is not
a specific query order. Thus the first triple in the query with ?property could also
be written as the last triple. Hence in this common example for ?property and the
remaining variables all the relations must be true before a result is obtained. If not all
relations are necessary to be true an optional graph pattern can be added (W3C 2013b).
An example showing this concept is found in the SPARQL query presented in Listing
3.10, which is based on the RDF data presented in Listing 3.9. The results of this query
is found in Table 3.6. Hence with optional graph pattern matching thesis:windowD2
is bound to variable ?window, despite it not being bound to ?width, since no triple
explicitly states this fact about thesis:windowD2.
1 SELECT ?element ?class ?superclass

3.4.2
Functions and modifiers
SPARQL has built-in functions and modifiers that helps build more specific and complex
queries. With the function BIND, variable bindings can be assigned to other variables
within the query pattern using the operator AS. The position of the BIND function in
the query pattern is of importance, since the preceding graph pattern is ended (W3C
2013b). Therefore it is not allowed to use any variables used in the preceding graph
pattern as the new variable in the BIND clause.
Aggregate functions are used to apply certain computations over a group of solutions
(W3C 2013b).
These functions are COUNT, SUM, MIN, MAX, AVG, GROUP_CONCAT, and
SAMPLE (W3C 2013b). In order to group solutions to a variable the syntax GROUP BY is
used, which functions as a modifier.
Functions supporting filtering and negation are supported in SPARQL with the basic
operators FILTER, regex, FILTER EXISTS, FILTER NOT EXISTS, MINUS, and bound (W3C
2013b). Inside a FILTER operator the constraints are expressed using for instance relation
operators or the regex function, which allows filtering for a specific string match. FILTER
EXISTS constraints the variable bindings by which only those bindings are allowed for
whom there exists the defined triple pattern in the clause. Similarly FILTER NOT EXISTS
test whether the pattern exists in the data and only if the pattern is not mached a
variable is bound i.e. a solution is established. MINUS removes triples from the dataset
and hence this operator can be used when certain data is to be omitted in the query.
Modifiers are commonly used outside the WHERE clause. For queries with many results
the LIMIT may be used, which sets a limit to the amount of results outputted. With
==PAGE:62==
44
3 Linked Data and The Semantic Web
OFFSET it is possible to define the index from where the result output should begin.
ORDER BY orders the solution sequence either in a ascending or descending order using
the optional modifiers ASC and DESC respectively (W3C 2013b).
In Listing 3.12 an
example SPARQL query is constructed using common functions to create a new variable
?requiredAir describing the required ventilation air-rate in each space. The dataset
that this query builds on is defined in Listing 3.11. The resulting variable bindings are
presented Table 3.7. Hence by using SPARQL and the simple function procedures new
data declarations can easily be found and created as new variables.

3.4.3
Property paths
SPARQL provides an operation for more efficient querying by the development of prop-
erty paths. With this operation both writing queries and the resulting variable bindings
are better structured. Property paths is used for finding a route between two connected
nodes, but by only specifying the properties leading from the subject to the object (W3C
2013b). Since this operation could be cyclic, restriction operators on the arbitrary path
length can be specified (W3C 2013b). Inverse path sequences are possible to define via
property paths in SPARQL using the operator ”ˆ”. An example of this concept is shown
in Listing 3.13 based on the ABox definition from Listing 3.3.
1 # Without property path
2 SELECT ?wall ?window
3 WHERE {
4
?wall thesis:hasoepening ?opening .
5
?opening thesis:isfilledby ?window .
6 }
7
8 # With property path
9 SELECT ?wall ?window

11
?wall thesis:hasopening/thesis:isfilledby ?window .

3.4.4
Remote datasets and SPARQL Endpoints
SPARQL is a protocol for RDF utilizing the HTTP (W3C 2013a). Two operations com-
prises the HTTP operations in SPARQL, namely a query operation and an update opera-
tion. Figure 3.12 illustrates the SPARQL Protocol Layer.
==PAGE:64==

3 Linked Data and The Semantic Web
A SPARQL Protocol service listens at a particular SPARQL endpoint and serves
HTTP requests from the SPARQL Protocol client (W3C 2013a).
These requests are
responded to by the SPARQL Protocol Server and the output results of the SPARQL
query are given in XML markup language documentation (Sack 2016f). Within such XML
file two distinct elements exists namely <head> and <results> (W3C 2013a). Inside
<head> the variable name are outputted, while <results> contain the variable bindings.

3.5 Reasoning engines and rulesets

Listing 3.14: XML results file for query in Listing 3.8.
In order to define, which dataset SPARQL should query the FROM and FROM NAMED
clauses are used. These points to the default graph and the named graphs in the RDF
dataset respectively. Federated queries are also possible with the SERVICE clause. Here
for each subquery in the graph pattern a URI is defined in SERVICE, which communicates
to the SPARQL Protocol to query the following triple pattern using the data specified
at this endpoint.
SPARQL is by default only concerned with the explicitly defined triple data. Hence
implicit knowledge retrieved by inference is not supported by default. However reasoning
engines supporting the OWL logic and linked to a SPARQL query engine provides
solutions to this. These reasoners are discussed in the following.
3.5
Reasoning engines and rulesets
Creating knowledge bases in a semantic format has already been established in this paper.
As previously mentioned to create a semantically rich and testable knowledge base the
TBox, ABox and RBox terminology should be present in the RDF dataset. Defining
and including rulesets allows for checking, finding dependencies and efficient querying
of the database via logical inference of present axioms (Pauwels et al. 2011). Research
in developing specialized rule-checking applications has been conducted in recent years
(Pauwels et al. 2016) (Zhang and Beetz 2016) (Costa and Pauwels 2015) (Pauwels et al.
2011).
However in order to launch the rulesets and also to query the implicit relations of
the information present, both a reasoning language and a reasoning engine are required
enabling parsing and inference over the data. The reasoning language deployed in the
Semantic Web and in most reasoning systems is primarily the computational logic-based
language OWL. Although OWL constitutes the basis of semantic reasoning it is limited
to a standard set complexity. Thus in order to create more complex reasoning a dedicated
rule-language is needed allowing for user-defined rules and complexity. Many alternatives
for both dedicated rule-languages and reasoning engines for the semantic web have been
developed in recent years (Pauwels et al. 2011). Most notable mentions for semantic rule-
languages are Notation 3 Logic (N3Logic), The Semantic Web Rule Language (SWRL),
and the SPIN SPARQL Syntax (SPIN), whereas the W3C standard Rule Interchange
Format (RIF) is a dedicated rule exchange standard (Pauwels and Zhang 2015) (Berners-
Lee 2008) (W3C 2004) (Knublauch 2011) (W3C 2001). RIF was introduced as a result
of the acknowledgment, that the development of one all-in-one rule-language, would lead
to less compatibility and quality of reasoning within the multitude of complex use-cases
(W3C 2001). Figure 3.13 illustrates the components of a reasoning-enabled rule-checking
system.
==PAGE:66==
48
3 Linked Data and The Semantic Web
