Figure 3.13: System and structure of rule-checking system.
3.5.1
Ruleset definition
The structure and syntax of semantic rule-definitions are based on logical inference of
IF-THEN statements. These are logical deductions consisting of a set of hypothetical
formulas (Pauwels et al. 2011). The IF part functions similarly to the SPARQL WHERE
clause, where a query pattern is evaluated on the basis of the axioms in the TBox and
assertional data in the ABox. This pattern matching either passes or fails with regards to
the asserted constraints and conditions. The THEN clause represents the logical deduction
or implication inferred by the hypothesis represented in the IF clause being matched to
the described subgraph. A simple example of a rule illustrating the basis of subgraph
pattern matching is seen in Figure 3.14. For this rule to launch an inference process
through the TBox and ABox a query is called. This could for instance be done in a
SPARQL query such as WHERE { ?s rdf:type thesis:door; thesis:area ?area }.
?s 
IF
THEN
thesis:width
thesis:height
thesis:door
?h
?w
a
?h*?w
?s
thesis:area
Figure 3.14: IF-THEN rule in Turtle format determining area on the basis of bindings
for width and height of door.
The SWRL rule-language adapts these principles, while N3Logic has a similar yet
alternative normative syntax (Pauwels et al. 2011). In the application of semantic rule-
definitions the logic of the chosen inference engine is of importance both with regards
to performance and also compatibility. Reasoning engines uses the facts of knowledge
base together with the defined IF-THEN rulesets to add conclusions (Al-Ajlan 2015). An
==PAGE:67==
3.5 Reasoning engines and rulesets
49
important characteristic of reasoners is the inference method applied to find solutions
to given logic. In relation to this characteristic two distinct logic-based approaches are
commonly used namely the backward- and forward-chaining (Pauwels et al. 2011.
3.5.2
Backward- and forward-chaining
Backward- and forward-chaining or reasoning are two distinct approaches for inference
engines to reason facts (Pauwels et al. 2011). Forward-chaining reasoners uses a bottom-
up model of inference (Al-Ajlan 2015). This means that it starts with the available facts
of the knowledge base and launches defined rules to infer new facts, adding these to
the knowledge base and the continues this process until no more facts can be reasoned
(Pauwels et al. 2011) (Al-Ajlan 2015). In contrast backward-chaining reasoners uses a
top-down inference model with starting point being the goal or hypothesis. Working
backwards from these goals or hypothesis the engine looks for rulesets and data in the
knowledge base supporting the hypothesis (Al-Ajlan 2015). This is done by looking in
the THEN clause of rules and checking whether the subjects of the hypothesis are included.
If a supporting rule is located the validity of the IF clause is checked and if at that given
point this clause is not known to be true, the IF part is included in the hypothesis and
the backward searching is continued (Pauwels et al. 2011).
Both of these approaches are best suitable for specific situations. Forward-chaining
is beneficial for reasoning of data, when the end-goal is not known. With this inference
method a substantial amount of information is inferred creating a better understanding
of the relational links between the data. However since the engine needs to check through
the entire database and rules to reach a goal, inefficient performance of even simple
queries is attached (Pauwels et al. 2011). Backward-chaining however is a goal-driven
approach, which is mostly suitable for situations, where the goals are known in advance.
This however causes for the inference procedure through the facts to be performed in a
more efficient manner. Thus both the choice of rule-language and inference logic impacts
the expressiveness and performance of rule-checking.
3.5.3
Rule-checking engines and applications
Many practical applications of rule-checking environments have emerged in later years
seeking to utilize the logic-based format of the semantic web on specific tasks within the
AEC domain (Pauwels and Zhang 2015). Examples revolving around building product
suggestions (Costa and Pauwels 2015), acoustic performance checking (Pauwels et al.
2011), and pre-checking and analysis of input data for energy performance (Kadolsky,
Baumgärtel, and Scherer 2014) all based their application on the use of inference rules.
Typically these engines implement a query processer together with an OWL reasoner
based on the description logic of OWL and a profile of certain expressiveness (Pauwels
et al. 2016). Of the various reasoning engines available a brief description is given in the
following of SPIN, EYE, and Stardog, which are some of the prominent engines used in
the given examples above.
==PAGE:68==
50
3 Linked Data and The Semantic Web
SPIN is a SPARQL syntax, which was developed in order to store SPARQL queries
together with data models and ontologies in RDF format (Knublauch 2011). SPIN
can be implemented in open-source APIs such as the Topbraid SPIN and Apache
Jena, which are both semantic web tools for modelling, testing and manipulation
of RDF models (The Apache Software Foundation 2017) (TopQuadrant 2017).
EYE implements the N3Logic rule-language and is a semi-backward reasoner meaning
that both backward and forward reasoning occurs in this engine (Pauwels et al.
2016).
Stardog is a semantic graph database supporting a large-scale triple store (Pauwels et
al. 2016). Stardog implements a built-in SPARQL query processer, which supports
SPARQL 1.1, together with OWL2 reasoning capabilities (Stardog 2017). Stardog
has a built-in notation for rule definitions utilizing the IF-THEN syntax together
with the Turtle RDF format, but also supports SWRL rules (Stardog 2017). In-
ference is processed through query rewritting with a closed-world reasoning engine
(Pauwels et al. 2016). Stardog and its latest version 4.2.3 runs on JAVA 8 and also
includes a HTTP protocol API allowing for both local and hosted service (Stardog
2017).
3.6
Sub-conclusion
The Semantic Web and Linked Data technologies is based on data representation in the
Resource Description Format (RDF) comprising of a directed labelled graph in triple
structure with a subject, predicate and object. Each element in the triple is identified
via a Universal Resource Identifier (URI) given each data element a unique identity.
RDF triples can be written in different syntaxes called serializations, where the Turtle
notation allows for prefix representation of namespaces for better readability of RDF
graphs.
In order to describe the semantics of data numerous knowledge vocabularies in the
form of ontologies is utilized for a richer semantic expressivitiy of data. Ontologies allows
for mapping data to a class or property, and further defines hierachy of data, equivalence
of different data, relationships, and constraints. Among many available ontologies the
most essentiel building blocks are found in the RDFS schema and in The Web Ontology
Langauge (OWL).
Different methods exists for linking RDF based data. These are namely the use of
(1) owl:equivalentClass to represent equivalence of classes from distinct ontologies,
(2) property definition with rdfs:domain and rdfs:range by which the subject and
object (class/literal) in a triple with the given property as predicate, are determined
by inference, and lastly (3) inheritance inference by the definition of rdfs:subClassOf,
where a instance being of a type inherits the given type’s superclass and properties.
SPARQL allows to query the RDF graphs and accessing data based on customization.
In order to access both explicitly stated data and any implicit relationship, reasoning
==PAGE:69==
3.6 Sub-conclusion
51
engines building upon the logic-based OWL ontology are implemented.
Customized
rules can be written for domain-specific tasks in several rule-formats based upon the
IF-THEN notation.
In the next chapter the third and finale part of the literature study will be presented
namely the investigation of building domain ontologies and especially the development
of an equivalent IFC ontology.
==PAGE:70==
52
==PAGE:71==
CHAPTER 4
Building Domain Ontologies
So far in this paper some examples have touched upon linked data implementations
within the AEC domain. One of more significant developments in this regard is the
conversion of the IFC standard and more specifically the EXPRESS language to a se-
mantic OWL ontology called ifcOWL. The scope of this third and finale chapter of the
literature study is to present the objective of such initiative, the developments within
this approach, the technical conversion procedure, and the practical usage and imple-
mentation ifcOWL.
4.1
Objective
Ontology development for a linked data representation of information and tasks within
the AEC domain has been researched extensively in later years. Linked data and Se-
mantic Web technologies would allow for efficient data interoperability, data exchange
and open-source application development due to the syntax of RDF, logic of OWL
and querying and reasoning capabilities of SPARQL (Pauwels and Terkaj 2016) (Farias,
Roxin, and Nicolle 2015) (Beetz, Leeuwen, and Vries 2009). This objective is not de-
tached from the original goal of the standardization and development of a schema for
AEC data exchange namely the IFC standard. IFC provides relational properties and
dependencies between entities, types etc. but in general lacks formal semantics and has
an limited range of expression (Pauwels and Terkaj 2016) (Pauwels et al. 2011). How-
ever IFC provides an industry standard with agreed-upon terminology, which benefits
the data exchange and interoperability between actors and their software. Other bar-
riers are that the IFC standard allows for numerous descriptions of the same data and
information, which complicates standardisation and classification within other systems,
partly because of this leading to ambiguities, but also since parties within the project
domain have different semantic comprehension of construction-related information.
Many initiatives hence bases their research on the fact that semantic web repre-
sentation of the IFC standard will provide a semantically rich data standard allowing
for open-source and independent application development supporting the tasks within
the AEC domain and extending the usage and exchange with other knowledge domains
(Pauwels and Terkaj 2016). This is possible due to (1) the directed labelled graphs
format represented by RDF with unique global identifiers in the use of URIs, (2) defin-
ing HTTP URIs so that others can look up and find the data, and also include it in
their ontology or data model, (3) the usage of RDF and existing logic-based ontologies
==PAGE:72==
54
4 Building Domain Ontologies
such as RDFS and OWL providing extensive semantic richness to resources, (4) making
datasets and ontologies available on the web with corresponding namespaces allowing
for SPARQL engines to access and query data, and (5) defining links to other sources
of data and datasets represented in RDF (Berners-Lee 2006) (Farias, Roxin, and Nicolle
2015). With these technologies and constructors available building data can be linked
to other data specific to GIS, materials, product manufacturers, energy performance,
classification systems, and for the use in technologies related to the Internet of Things
(IoT) (Pauwels 2015).
4.2
AEC ontologies
The most notable research of ontology development within the AEC domain is the
suggestion of a ifcOWL ontology by Pauwels and Terkaj (2016) as a result of a joint-
effort within the BuildingSMART Linked Data Working Group (BuildingSMART 2016c).
Previous proposals among others were suggested by Hoang and Törmä (Hoang and
Törmä), Barbau et al. (2012), Beetz and De Vries (2009), Krima et al. (2009), Schevers
and Drogemuller (2006). Hence the current ifcOWL ontology is not a product of stand-
alone research, but have been modified and further specified by the previous research
conducted. Some extensions to ifcOWL have been developed such as ifcWoD suggested
by Farias, Roxin, and Nicolle (2015), which is an adaption allowing for simplification
of querying and optimization of query execution time. Another initiative is simpleBIM,
which restructures and simplifies the ifcOWL ontology so that RDF representation of
IFC models are less complex and more usable for practical application (Pauwels and
Roxin 2016).
A part from the research and development of a IFC ontology many generic ontologies
exists, which describes building data more or less. The DBpedia ontology is by far the
most structured effort for making information of the web available in a semantic-based
knowledge graph (Lehmann et al. 2015). Since the data of this knowledge base is directly
linked from online encyclopedia Wikipedia this semantic database covers a wide variety
of domains providing describition of around 4.58 million things in just the english ver-
sion (DBpedia 2017). DBpedia includes class and property axioms related to a building,
such as dbo:Building with superclass dbo:ArchitecturalStructure, dbo:Tower and
dbo:Arena, property classes specific to the building structure dbo:floorArea, but also
to hold more general information in for instance dbo:rebuildingYear. Ontologies rep-
resenting more specific axioms related to building and building-design are found in the
SEMANCO ontology providing a OWL2 based ontology related to energy management,
urban planning and carbon reduction (SEMANCO 2014). The development of semantic
web tools for tasks related to buildings and design are primarily kept to be task-specific.
Other more domain-specific ontologies and applications have been developed in relation
to research projects. The research conducted by Wicaksono et al. (2013) produced a
ontology for intelligent energy management, where IFC entities were mapped to OWL
and the native knowledge base. This ontology together with SWRL rules allowed for
==PAGE:73==
4.3 IFC to ifcOWL conversion
55
monitoring and checking of building-energy elements such as heaters, switches etc. A
combination of BIM and semantic web technologies was developed for building heritage
management by Pauwels et al. (2013).
This application promises to provide a man-
agement system with improved understanding of cultural diversity and environmental
sustainability. A third application and development was presented by Corry et al. (2015),
where a performance assessment ontology was developed for environmental and energy
management of buildings. The ontology developed focused on linking of heterogeneous
sources of data to structure performance analysis.
In this thesis the scope is to investigate and utilize the ifcOWL ontology for knowledge-
base creation, querying and reasoning. Hence in the following part the technicalities of
the conversion procedure from the IFC schema to an ifcOWL ontology by Pauwels and
Terkaj (2016) is presented. Furthermore the executive summary presented in Pauwels,
Terkaj, and Beetz (2015) is used for this purpose. Only the most essentiel parts of the
conversion procedure are handled and further specifications are found in Pauwels and
Terkaj (2016). The IFC4 Addendum 1 is used as the base version for conversion in this
proposal. However this conversion procedure is applicable on IFC2x3, IFC2x3 Technical
Corrigendum 1, and IFC4 (Pauwels, Terkaj, and Beetz 2015).
4.3
IFC to ifcOWL conversion
4.3.1
Criteria and definition
The ifcOWL ontology development is based on three criteria (Pauwels and Terkaj 2016):
1. The ifcOWL ontology is required to be in OWL2 DL.
2. The ifcOWL ontology should follow and match EXPRESS as closely as possible.
3. The ifcOWL ontology has a primary goal of supporting conversion of IFC instance
files into equivalent RDF files.
The first and most important criteria is the choice of OWL2 semantic profile. The
authors base their choice of OWL2 DL on the rich expressiveness of DL, whereby the
first criteria supports the second-most important criteria namely the ontology matching
the original EXPRESS schema as closely as possible. As a result of criteria one and two
the performance of reasoning engines working with ifcOWL would be decreased, however
the semantic expressivity is maintained. The third criterion is based on the fact, which
has been touched upon previously in this paper, that mostly IFC models are shared in
AEC projects. Hence the need to create a RDF graph of a IFC model from scratch using
ifcOWL is not of practical importance. As a result the RULE and FUNCTION declarations
in the IFC standard are not handled in this conversion. This decision is based on (1)
that these declarations restricts the content of a IFC file, and (2) the restrictions from
these declarations are typically checked within the generation of a IFC file and hence the
==PAGE:74==
56
4 Building Domain Ontologies
converted RDF graph would comply with these restrictions (Pauwels and Terkaj 2016).
Further research on this topic has been presented by Terkaj and Šojić (2015).
For each EXPRESS schema corresponding to an IFC standard an ifcOWL ontology is
created with a unique URI. The namespace URI proposed is http://www.buildingsmart-
tech.org/ifcOWL/[schemaName], where the schema name correspond to name of the
IFC standard such as IFC4_ADD1. In contrast to using one version-independent URI for
the ifcOWL ontology, this approach creates more well-defined axiom definitions. Back-
ward compatibility exists within the EXPRESS schema for IFC, but for safer implemen-
tation relational constraints could be added to each ifcOWL ontology representing a
given IFC version. In Listing 4.1 the ontologies implemented in the ifcOWL proposal
are listed.
1 @base <http://www.buildingsmart -tech.org/ifcOWL/IFC4_ADD1> .
2 @prefix : <http://www.buildingsmart -tech.org/ifcOWL/IFC4_ADD1> .
3 @prefix ifcowl: <http://www.buildingsmart -tech.org/ifcOWL/IFC4_ADD1> .
4 @prefix xsd: <http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema#> .
5 @prefix owl: <http://www.w3.org/2002/07/owl#> .
6 @prefix rdfs: <http://www.w3.org/2000/01/rdf-schema#> .
7 @prefix dce: <http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1> .
8 @prefix rdf: <http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#> .
9 @prefix vann: <http//purl.org/vocab/vann/> .
10 @prefix cc: <http://creativecommons.org/ns#> .
11 @prefix express: <http://purl.org/voc/express#> .
12 @prefix list: <http://www.co-ode.org/ontologies/list.owl#> .
Listing 4.1: Ontologies implemented in ifcOWL ontology suggested in Pauwels and
Terkaj (2016).
4.3.2
Data type declarations
The EXPRESS schema operates with both simple and named datypes. Due to the simple
datatypes, such as NUMBER, REAL, INTEGER, LOGICAL, BOOLEAN, STRING, and BINARY,
being a inherent part of the EXPRESS schema, these datatypes are handled in the
EXPRESS ontology (express:) rather than directly in the ifcOWL ontology (ifcowl:).
Listing 4.2 shows the definition of NUMBER and REAL, which applies a restriction on the
values of the property express:hasDouble being of xsd:double.

4.3.3
Aggregations, lists and enumerations
Aggregation data types in EXPRESS are LIST, SET, and ARRAY.
Lists represents
ordered sequences of data and while the rdfs ontology provides a syntax for writ-
ing ordered lists i.e.
using rdf:Seq, rdf:List, rdf:first and rdf:rest in either
a collection or container structure, the ifcOWL ontology relies on the list: ontol-
ogy.
Different list structures are then created using express: in accordance with
what datatype the list include.
In express:, classes of lists are present, such as
express:INTEGER_List, express:STRING_List etc. which are defined as subclasses
of list:OWLList. Furthermore cardinality restrictions are defined for object proper-
ties for these lists such as list:hasContents, list:isFollowedBy, and list:hasNext.
Listing 4.4 highlights list definitions in the ifcOWL ontology by showing the repre-
sentation of ifcowl:IfcSurfaceTexture_List. The list:hasContents defines that
only instances belonging to ifcowl:IfcSurfaceTexture are allowed in this list. list:
hasNext and list:isFollowed are properties restricted to be instances of ifcowl:
IfcSurfaceTexture_List, whereby each element in the list has a defined relation to
the next and following element.
1 ifcowl:IfcSurfaceTexture_List
2
a
owl:Class ;
3
rdfs:subClassOf
list:OWLList ;
4

[ a
owl:Restriction ;
5
owl:allValuesFrom
ifcowl:IfcSurfaceTexture_List ;

4.3.4
Entities and attributes
The foundation of the IFC schema lies in the named entities and attributes definitions.
Entities such as ifcowl:IfcWallStandardCase, ifcowl:IfcPipeSegment etc. are con-
verted to common OWL classes with any subclasses defined by rdfs:subClassOf. Ab-
stract supertypes are declared as a subclass of the union of its subclasses defined by
owl:unionOf (Pauwels and Terkaj 2016).
Disjointness of entities is defined through
owl:disjointWith.
Attributes are basically defined as owl:ObjectProperty, but additional constraints
are possible as previously discussed with lists. Another important feature in ifcOWL is
the naming convention of properties in order to define unique URIs. Each object property
has exactly one data type in both rdfs:range and rdfs:domain and hence the naming
convention should allow to define this properly. This is done by the following syntax
ifcowl:propertyName_IfcClassName. In order to highlight the entity declaration and
definition of properties, Listing 4.6 shows the definition of ifcowl:IfcPipeSegment
==PAGE:77==
4.3 IFC to ifcOWL conversion
59
with class and subclass definitions, property restrictions and disjointness declarations.
The second subclass definition operates as a restriction, which constraints the property
class ifcowl:predefinedType_IfcPipeSegment to have one value as would also be
defined in the rdfs:range for this property. This states that an instance of a ifcowl:
IfcPipeSegment can only be of one type at a given moment.

Listing 4.6: Definition of ifcowl:IfcPipeSegment with property restrictions and
disjointness with other classes.
Inverse attribute declarations are common in the IFC schema and the definition of
such relations is done through the OWL property owl:inverseOf. In using a constructor
such as owl:inverseOf the syntax of the schema should be thoroughly investigated. If
a given attribute or property class in this case has two or more inverse properties an
OWL reasoner would infer these properties to be equivalent. As exemplified in Pauwels
and Terkaj (2016) the attribute RelatedDefinitions of entity IfcRelDeclares has
inverse attribute HasContext of the entity IfcObjectDefinition. Furthermore it has
inverse attribute HasContext of entity IfcPropertyDefinition. In these cases inverse
properties should be ignored and not defined. Another case where inverse attributes are
to be ignored is for regular attribute or its inverse having LIST or ARRAY as the range.
An example of defining inverse attributes in the ifcOWL ontology is presented in Listing
4.7 for the property ifcowl:hasFillings_IfcOpeningElement.

4.4
Conversion of IFC model to RDF graph
Using the axioms defined in the ifcOWL ontology one must define a conversion procedure
for creating a RDF-based graph model of a IFC model. A demo conversion tool developed
by Pauwels and Oraskeri (2015) is publicly available and allows for direct conversion of
any IFC-STEP file to a RDF graph based upon the ifcOWL ontology in Turtle syntax.
4.4.1
Generation of instance data (ABox)
Using the conversion tool (Pauwels and Oraskeri 2015) a distinct namespace URI @prefix
inst: <http://linkedbuildingdata.net/ifc/resources[yearmonthdate_hourminsec]
/> is utilized for the expression of instance data representing the IFC model elements.
Hence for each time the IFC model is converted to RDF the URI changes and corre-
sponds to the given time and date of conversion. Furthermore the suffix of the instances
is generated based upon the #identification-numbers of the IFC-STEP file. In practice
this would mean that two conversions of the same model from IFC-to-RDF would yield
different URIs for the same entity. As a result the entities in the IFC model are not
given a global uniqueness, since for each conversion their identification i.e. URI changes.
This concept and its implications will be further discussed later in this paper.
4.4.2
Entity representation
In Listing 4.8 an example is presented, which contains a representation of an instance of
IfcWallStandardCase in both STEP and Turtle format. In general each of the definitions
in the STEP file of IfcWallStandardCase-101261 is converted to an instance in RDF and
then declared to be a certain rdf:type e.g. the instance inst:IfcGloballyUniqueId_
190046 is of type ifcowl:IfcGloballyUniqueId. The value of these definitions are
then found associated with this instance and are represented using the express: ontol-
ogy e.g. inst:IfcLabel_190047 has express:hasString value "BasicWall:Generic-
200mm:398".

4.4.3
Property sets and single values
A property set related to a given entity-instance is defined through the definition of in-
stances of class ifcowl:IfcRelDefinesByProperties. To these instances the property
set instance and related object instance are attached respectively via predicates:
• ifcowl:relatingPropertyDefinition_IfcRelDefinesByProperties
• ifcowl:relatedObjects_IfcRelDefines
==PAGE:80==
62
4 Building Domain Ontologies
The property set definitions are defined as instances of IfcPropertySingleValue
and via the property class ifcowl:hasProperties_IfcPropertySet attached to the
respective property set. Hence a hierarchy is present in the interrelation between the
nodes describing an objects property definitions. A graphical illustration of property set
definitions and the connection between the property set "Pset_StairCommon" and stair
instance inst:IfcStair_2026 is presented in Figure 4.1.
4.4.4

Other property sets definitions are represented as property single values with datatype
IfcLengthMeasure or IfcPositiveLengthMeasure.
Since this conversion procedure
implements the list definitions list:hasContents and list:hasNext a combination of
instances of ifcowl:IfcLengthMeasure and ifcowl:IfcLengthMeasure_List are used.
Other more complicated and extensive list structures are found in IfcCartesianPoint
and especially for determining coordinates of these. Here the IfcLengthMeasure combi-
nation can be used, but also a corresponding usage of instances of IfcCartesianPoint
and IfcCartesianPoint_List.
Object placements of elements are also determined by these constructors. Listing 4.9

4.4.5
Relational constructors
In the context of property sets the IfcRelDefinesByProperties was covered previously.
However many other relational constructors from the IFC schema provides semantic defi-
nitions for entity interrelation. However with the RDF syntax these are better accessible.
With the proposed conversion procedure this point is valid, although depending on the
constructor in question an excessive amount of triples needs to be queried through for
finding basic data. In the following only the end-result of the conversion from EXPRESS
to RDF is illustrated for some of these constructors to highlight the principle.
4.4.5.1
IfcRelVoidsElement and IfcRelFillsElement
One batch of relation constructors in ifcOWL that creates 1-1 relations, and allows for
consistent and easy determination of links are the IfcRelVoidsElement and IfcRelFillsElement.
As can be seen from Listing 4.10 the inst:IfcOpeningElement_73154 relates to both
the wall through the void-relation, and also to the window through the fills-relation.
Hence each of these void- and fill-element instances are connected through occurrence
of the same opening-element. Thus using simple SPARQL operations it is possible to
search trough the database and find both specific and generic data related to these
element-opening-fill relationships.
1 inst:IfcRelVoidsElement_73159

inst:
IfcOpeningElement_73154 .

4.5
Sub-conclusion
An ontology representation of the IFC schema is developed by Pauwels and Terkaj
(2016) providing a semantic profile of the underlying EXPRESS language. The ontology
provides extensive class, property and relational definitions. The conversion of IFC to
RDF is only one-way meaning that changes made to the RDF cannot as of now be
converted back to IFC or EXPRESS. The conversion tool developed by Pauwels and
Oraskeri (2015) is utilized in this thesis. Although the conversion tool provides efficient
and easy mapping from the EXPRESS schema to an equivalent rich semantic RDF
graph it does have practical limitations. As a well-known fact iterations, modifications
and changes to project information and hence supporting BIM models occurs several
times over in AEC projects. Since this conversion tool provides a one-way mapping
from IFC to RDF maintaining data consistency is an issue. This is namely due to the
unique URIs of entities in the model being changed during each conversion, which then
requires time and resources for keeping an order of these changes.
However for the
tasks of classification and identification of building model entities in a static format this
demo conversion tool is sufficient. The practical implications of this procedure will be
discussed later in this paper.
This chapter and the preceding two chapters about BIM standards and work-flows
and Semantic Web and Linked Data technologies concludes the literature study findings
of the thesis. In the following chapter the process and findings of the industry survey
conducted with Danish consulting company ALECTIA will be presented.

CHAPTER 5
Industry Survey
This chapter describes the survey conducted with ALECTIA’s BIM department, where
the main focus was to understand the practical application and challenges within the
use of CCS. However to get a more holistic and reflective view on the subject of building
classification and ALECTIA’s approach to such tasks, the questions were not restricted
to CCS alone. The full interview was conducted in Danish and can be found in audio
format in Appendix B and a partially transcribed version in C.
5.1
Interview Guide
Prior to the survey an interview guide has been developed. The guide has been divided
into four stages, where each stage represents a subject of interest. The interview guide
increases in taxonomy for each stage. In the following overview a subset of the different
types of questions for each stage are highlighted.
Stage one - The interview person
• How many years of experience do you have in the AEC-industry?
• What are your key responsibilities?
Stage two - The general use of classification systems in Alectia
• Which classification systems are generally used in projects in Alectia?
• What is the goal of implementing CCS for Alectia?
• How do you classify with CCS?
Stage three - Process, collaboration and management
• How do you coordinate with other disciplines?
• How do you handle types? Does it differ from one project to another?
Stage four - Improvement potentials
• Which technologies could improve or automate the use of CCS?
==PAGE:86==
68
5 Industry Survey
• Do you see a potential in applying Semantic Web principles in the implementation
of CCS and in the usage of BIM in general?
The guide was planned to structure the interview, but was not intended to be strictly
followed. Mainly in order to give the interview a natural flow of speech, but also to give
the interview person the possibility to speak about other related matters, which could
be of interest.
5.2
interview Attendees
In Table 5.1 the interview attendees at Alectia are shown.
Name
Profession
Asger Wahlquist Petersen
BIM supervisor
Mads Holten Rasmussen
HVAC engineer and Industrial Ph.d
Table 5.1: Interview attendees at Alectia.
Asger Wahlquist Petersen is an architectural construction manager, who currently
functions as a BIM supervisor in ALECTIA’s BIM department. Asger develops BIM
models, prepares and produces tender-lists, and maintains consistency between the clas-
sification codes in the BIM model and project documents. Mads Holten Rasmussen is a
civil engineer at ALECTIA and currently enrolled as a Industrial Phd. student at the
Technical University of Denmark focusing on application of linked data and semantic
web principles in the AEC domain.
The questions in stage two and three were primarily related to ALECTIA’s practices
and working methods with classification. Stage four questions were related to improve-
ment potentials and more specific towards semantic web and linked data principles,
which demanded some introduction to and knowledge about these principles, and hence
Mads Holten Rasmussen was invited to attend the interview.
5.3
Findings
The Danish AEC domain is not restricted to use one specific classification system in AEC
projects. Various classification systems can be used for different purposes depending on
the project owner’s requirements. ALECTIA has worked with four different classifica-
tion systems, dependent on the project and specific team-agreements, namely SfB, CCS,
FK and BIM7aa, where the latter only has been used in one project so far.
“In ALECTIA the SfB system is the standard classification system, but we are currently
trying to convert to CCS, since it is a requirement in the new ICT specifications”
==PAGE:87==
5.3 Findings
69
5.3.1
CCS in Alectia
According to Asger CCS is used by ALECTIA with the intent to ensure consistency
between the tender documents, models and descriptions. In a project life-cycle CCS is
first considered in the project proposal, where the structure of the tender documents
and bill of quantities is specified. CCS is mainly used in the main project, where clas-
sification codes are assigned in the BIM model. In order to ensure the quality of the
classification in the model, quantity-takeoffs are extracted and compared with the initial
tender documents. This comparison leads to possible corrections and these are checked
and coordinated manually together with the domain-specific experts. ALECTIA has yet
to utilize CCS in maintenance- and operation phases.
ALECTIA does not use CCS to a full extent, since only CCS topnodes, single-level
IDs, type-IDs, and multi-level IDs are assigned to building elements.
In the tender
documents the following columns are assigned a value if applicable:
• CCS_ClassCode
• CCS_SingleLevelID
• CCS_TypeID
• CCS_MultiLevelID
• Description of components
The use of CCS in ALECTIA does not include the CCS singe-level location-at ID of
each element, since this has not been a requirement from project-owners in the projects
so far.
5.3.2
Software tools
Different software-tools specific for classification have been implemented and tested by
ALECTIA in the work with CCS. Amongst these are Standard Project Information
Exchange (Spine) and Classify, which are both supported as Autodesk Revit plugins
and developed to assist the end-user with classification assignment. According to Asger,
Spine and Classify are limited in their functionality in regards to the specific needs of
ALECTIA.
“We have looked a lot at Spine and we have looked a lot at Classify, but it is not
something we are using in Alectia. This is because it is minimal what the programs are
able to in comparison to what we need here. They only give us the class code and it is
simple to come up with the object without. It is not here we see the challenge.”
Instead of using these softwares ALECTIA is currently working on creating a database,
where all the typical building components are classified and ready to use. The idea is
then to use the classification codes and through programming determine any combined
classification IDs for the given types.
==PAGE:88==
70
5 Industry Survey
5.4
Challenges
5.4.1
Manual Process
One of the main challenges is that the process of classification assigning in general is
manually driven, which makes it static and non-open to changes. The tasks dealing with
the assigning of classification codes are simple, but remain open to mistakes, errors and
deficiencies, since human involvement is present. Every time different domains change,
add or remove components, the changes in the CCS classification will not automatically
be registered. The domains are then obliged to manually assign the CCS codes to the
changed components.
5.4.2
Collaboration
According to Asger the main challenges lies within the consistency and unnecessary it-
eration of the classification of elements. Even though these issues are related to the
project management they have had an affect on other domains. It has been experienced
in greater projects, when different domains have used several Autodesk Revit files con-
taining the same components. This has caused confusion amongst the domains and
duplications of classification codes, which have resulted in unnecessary correction and
workload.
5.4.3
Structure & content of tender documents
Although the documents related to the tender phase are mainly the same, the structure
and content of the documents differ. A new structure is created at project start and
according to Asger this is time consuming.
“we are starting from scratch every time (…) We develop a new system every time we
start a new project… And this is time consuming.”
Especially the structure of the tender lists is experienced to differ.
“It would help us a lot with standardizing our way of work if the way they offer things
and the way they divide things on a tender list were uniform”
Although the general structure of the tender lists are the same the differences are
still challenging and causes manual assigning of classification codes to the components.
5.4.4
Identification Numbers
The identification numbers included in the CCS codes can cause room for ambiguity
between the different domains from project to project. In Alectia are the identification
==PAGE:89==
5.5 Optimization
71
numbers a part of a self-made project-specific system, where components are represent-
ing different categories within the same types.
”We categorize the classes with the identification number, which makes it possible for
us to differ between the classes. For example if we are talking about walls: Walls with
the identifaction numbers between 500 and 600 are elemented walls, while walls with
the numbers between 600 and 700 are in-situ.”
5.5
Optimization
The ICT specifications are not requiring the full use of CCS from project start to de-
molition. It only requires a way of structuring data and thereby having coherence in
the tender documents. Alectia has not yet been involved in projects, where the CCS
location ID has been required. This creates a limitation in Alectia’s capacity whenever
a project owner requires the use of the CCS location ID.
Alectia is currently developing an internal database consisting of CCS classified build-
ing components in order to deal with some of the manual work. The purpose of this
database is not solely to classify according to CCS but also to make the tender docu-
ments and data more structured. According to Asger will this assist in an automation
of some of the current manual labor within the classification assigning. According to
Mads it is perhaps the approach and classification codes, which needs changing. The
classification codes need to be more domain-specific and pointed at the stakeholder who
needs the exact desired information.
“Different domains have different ways at interpreting things, which creates thousand
ways to look and regard at a building and its components. This is hard to classify with
a single classification code. The classification code needs to be domain-specific. The
linked data approach is a good solution to this ”
Mads believes that the linked data approach will solve a lot of the issues dealing
with the classification codes. As an example can a system be regarded differently by the
domains. The HVAC domain will regard a zone as supply and demand of air, where the
fire safety domain will look at a zone as a fire section with escape routes. These zones
may not have the same size and shape, why it is crucial, according to Mads, that the
classification codes need to be more domain-specific.
5.6
Sub-Conclusion
The use of classification in the Danish AEC industry is not restricted to the use of CCS
only, nevertheless are the ICT regulations prone to the use of CCS. Although the most
common used classification system is SfB is ALECTIA undergoing a conversion to CCS
==PAGE:90==
72
5 Industry Survey
in order to meet the requirements of the ICT regulations. In projects where CCS have
been used are only some aspects of CCS Identification included since the other aspects
have not been required from the client. ALECTIA’s future focus will be on implementing
as many aspects of CCS as possible to widen the scope of a client’s potential demands.
ALECTIA have tried to use software tools as Spine and Classify to deal with the manual
labor related to the classification assigning, but was soon realized not capable of solving
the issues and challenges ALECTIA are facing. Asger mentioned, that Spine and CLas-
sify only were able to assign Class Codes. It may have been a deprecated or an older
version of the software tools, since the current versions of both Spine and Classify are
able to assign both CCS classification and some aspects of CCS identification.
ALECTIA are currently looking for other solutions to deal with the challenges.
A
database consisting of already classified components, where the idea is to use algorithms
and programming to partially automate the classification tasks in the BIMs.
==PAGE:91==
CHAPTER 6
Product
This chapter will present the proposed solution for automating CCS classification of
building models utilizing the IFC-to-RDF conversion tool based upon the ifcOWL on-
tology and semantic web technologies. Furthermore the thoughts behind the different
aspects of the proposal will be discussed and the technical and practical implications
will be analyzed.
6.1
Overview and components
The objective of the proposed product solution is to utilize the entity and property
declarations in the IFC schema, the semantic relations developed through the ifcOWL
ontology, and customized rulesets for classifying and identifying building elements in the
BIM model with regards to CCS. The product is a workflow and requires certain active
steps from the end-user in order to reach the solution. In general the product revolves
around five categories namely, (1) a BIM model, (2) IFC-to-RDF conversion tool, (3)
ontologies and rulesets developed specifically for CCS classification, (4) a graph database
supporting SPARQL and reasoning and (5) a back-end application functioning mainly
as a running server with user-interface that displays the results based upon back-end
computing. Figure 6.1 gives a more detailed overview of the product and flow. Also this
overview illustrates the technologies and software utilized by the authors for product
development.
In the following subsections a subset of the five categories and corresponding tech-
nology/software will be elaborated upon. A more detailed description of the intended
workflow behind the product will be presented in the section of this chapter revolving
around practical application.
6.1.1
BIM model
The authors have utilized Autodesk Revit for the task of model development, since this
is a commonly-used BIM software in Denmark and furthermore is the primary tool used
by Alectia. In general the only requirement that the chosen BIM software must fulfill
is the support of IFC export. Hence all BIM software-tools supporting IFC export are
applicable. The CCS ontology is built upon IFC4 definitions and hence it is suggested
to convert the model to this IFC format, although IFC2x3 functions properly as well.
Since only one IFC file can be parsed by the IFC-to-RDF converter tool at a time, it
==PAGE:92==
74
6 Product
ifcOWL 
CCS ontology
CCS rulesets
IFC-to-RDF

+

Figure 6.1: Overview of product flow and technologies corresponding to the five cate-
gories, (1) the BIM model, (2) the IFC-to-RDF conversion tool, (3) ontolo-
gies and rulesets, (4) the graph database, and (5) the back-end application
and server.
is suggested to combine the sub-disciplinary models to one shared model. This will be
further discussed later in this chapter.
6.1.2
Ontologies and rulesets
After the building model has been exported to IFC and then to RDF, a semantic profile
of the BIM model is hence available including IFC entities, attributes and relations. The
authors have developed an ontology for CCS classification, which includes property and
class definitions utilized for mapping IFC entities, more specifically building elements
and distribution elements, to corresponding CCS class-codes, topnodes and class-names.
In addition to the CCS ontology a separate ”ontology” is developed comprising of rule-
sets, which provides further advanced methods for CCS classification. Besides the CCS
ontology and CCS rulesets the ifcOWL ontology can be utilized for further semantic
reasoning, since in it lies the definitions of the classes, indviduals, properties, enumera-
tions. The details of the approach is presented in the section regarding development of
the CCS ontology and rulesets. Together with the developed ontology and rulesets the
ifcOWL ontology is also included in the project RDF database.
==PAGE:93==
6.2 Development of CCS ontology and rulesets
75
6.1.3
Graph database
The Stardog semantic graph database triple-store is implemented for querying and rea-
soning of the data comprising of the RDF based building model, CCS ontology, and
CCS rulesets. Stardog is chosen due to it being a high-performance query engine and
its reasoning abilities with support of OWL2 reasoning. For these tasks the community-
version is installed allowing for 25 millions triples per database with a maximum of 10
databases. Stardog is accessed and communicated to through the back-end application
server.
6.1.4
Back-end application
For making the results of the CCS classification available in the project and also to
compute the identification IDs, which is not readily possible through just semantic web
tools, both a web-server and a back-end application needs to implemented. The authors
have utilized Cloud9 for these tasks, which includes tools for running a hosted server
and to develop a back-end application. Cloud9 is a cloud-based application tool based
upon the JavaScript runtime environment Node.js, which allows for creating and running
server tools and applications. The specifications of the back-end application is 2.5 GB
of RAM and disk-size of 10 GB. A more detailed overview of developing the host server
and back-end for CCS classification and results-handling will be provided in a separate
section of this chapter.
6.2
Development of CCS ontology and
rulesets
The key components of the product is the CCS ontology and rulesets developed for the
task of mapping IFC entities to CCS equivalent classes and also utilizing the semantic
relations available in the RDF graph. Both the ontology and rulesets are written in Tur-
tle syntax with the rulesets written as IF-THEN statements compliant with the Stardog
reasoner syntax. The two entities i.e. the ontology and rulesets, functions together in
order to determine the CCS mapping. The full CCS ontology and rulesets developed
can be found in Appendix D and E respectively.
In the considerations of the approach of mapping IFC entities to CCS classes four
main approaches were considered:
1. IFC entity mapping in which 1-1 relations are made between IFC entities and
CCS class information such as top-node, class-name and class-code using property
restrictions
==PAGE:94==
76
6 Product
2. Creating an owl:Class for each CCS equivalent entity and using property restric-
tions to attach the CCS top-node, class-code and class-name. Furthermore to map
the CCS class to a common IFC entity using equivalence operators.
3. Determining CCS classes based upon investigation of entity properties such as type
properties using string matching
4. Triple pattern matching in rulesets, where each entity if available in the graph is
mapped to a CCS class in the THEN clause
Although approach 1 functions in the same manner as approach 2 in a close-world
assumption, it is insufficient with regards to extensibility and matching with other clas-
sification systems, since it does not provide a single unique class representing a CCS
entity. In approach 2 however CCS entities are represented as classes and using the
owl:equivalentClass mapped to relevant IFC entities. This approach is chosen for
the ontology part of the solution. However many building objects with regards to classi-
fication are determined through their properties. CCS operates with constructive com-
ponents in the underground and outside the underground. Mapping these 1-1 with IFC
is not possible without implementing rulesets allowing for property search and triple
pattern matching, since in IFC a beam whether part of the foundation system is rep-
resented as a IfcBeam. Hence for these purposes a combination of approach 3 and 4
is applied although with some modifications. These two approaches are utilized in the
CCS rulesets.
In the CCS ontology the CCS top-node, class-code and class-name are determined.
The CCS rulesets allows for determining the CCS multi-level ID, multi-level type-ID,
and single-level location-at-ID. Since RDF functions as a triple syntax and not as a
list-structure the identification procedure and hence determination of CCS single-level
ID and type ID cannot be computed using RDF and SPARQL alone. This is therefore
done in the back-end application. Despite the primary identification processes being
conducted by the back-end application, determining CCS single-level ID for IfcBuilding,
IfcStorey and IfcSpace is done in the CCS rulesets.
A more technical and detailed
overview of the ontology and rulesets is given in the following. Furthermore the details
of the identification process and back-end application will be provided in a separate
section in this chapter.
6.2.1
Ontology definition
As previously mentioned CCS provides three main categories for defining building el-
ements namely as a functional system, technical system or as a component.
These
categories are intended for specific phases of the project, where with the increase of
detailing over time the designers and engineers also look to define their elements accord-
ingly. Creating an equivalent class for CCS entities from all three categories can be
readily done, however the challenge lies in mapping to a common IFC entity. In CCS
a ’wall’ can be described by both a ’Wall System (B)’, a ’Wall Structure (AD)’, and a
==PAGE:95==
6.2 Development of CCS ontology and rulesets
77
’Wall Construction (BD)’. However IFC only operates with IfcWallStandardCase (being
the most common type) and IfcWallElementedCase both being subtype of IfcWall. As
a consequence it is difficult to map the CCS classes to one IFC entity, since this would
create a multitude of classification codes. For instance mapping ’Wall System (B)’ to
IfcWall would yield that IfcWallStandardCase and IfcWallElementedCase are also cate-
gorized as ’Wall System (B)’, due to their definition in the ifcOWL ontology as being
rdfs:subClassOf ifcowl:IfcWall. Also a curtain-wall could be included in these defi-
nitions. Furthermore during a project the intention is to only have one code per element
i.e. a single wall instance cannot be ’AD’ and ’BD’ at the same time. Hence defining an
ontology covering all possibilities and phases of a project is difficult and the technologies
supporting the definition of ontologies does not have these capabilities readily available.
As a result the authors have chosen to develop the ontology and rulesets with the initial
phases of the project in mind. These phases defined by ISO 29481-1 could include the
pre-construction phases (International Organization for Standardization 2016).
To mitigate this concept of multiple CCS class memberships for one IFC entity, only
one CCS class can be mapped to one distinct IFC entity. For instance for walls this would
include both IfcWallStandardCase, IfcWallElementedCase, and IfcCurtainWall and these
are categorized as ’Wall Structure (AD)’. Hence since the three codes ’B’, ’AD’, and ’BD’
besides defining the wall itself also holds information of the proposed phase of the project,
the CCS ontology only functions with one code. Using this approach it is thus sensible to
go down in hierarchy of entities and establish the relation. In the CCS system the three
levels of categories do not typically all cover the same ’element’. Walls exists as seen
both as a functional system ’B’ and as two distinct technical systems ’AD’ and ’BD’, but
not on component level. One of the goals of this investigation and resulting solution is
to be of practical value, and since elements are typically covered in the technical systems
and component categories the proposed solution will primarily cover these two levels and
henceforth omit the functional system category. Future developments of the ontology
can include the different phases and categories of CCS.
6.2.2
Property definitions
In Table 6.1 the property classes defined in the CCS ontology are listed. All properties
are defined as of rdf:type owl:DatatypeProperty, since the rdfs:range representing
the codes or names is a written as strings. The choice of rdfs:domain being ifcowl:
IfcProduct is based upon it being an abstract supertype of ifcowl:IfcElement and
ifcowl:IfcSpatialStructureElement. From these building elements, distributions el-
ements and spaces are defined. Property classes specific to spaces are correspondingly de-
fined with ifcowl:IfcSpace as domain. These property classes are defined and included
for future usage and reference, whereas in this product proposal the focus will be on
ccs:topNode, ccs:classCode, ccs:className, ccs:singleLevelID, and ccs:typeID
for the definition of single entities. For the definition of combined- and location-IDs the
relevant property classes included are ccs:multiLevelID, ccs:multiLevelTypeID, and
ccs:singleLevelLocationAtID.

property restrictions logic is implemented using owl:Restriction.

the case presented with ccs:WallStructure mapped to ifcowl:IfcWallStandardCase,
ifcowl:IfcWallElementedCase and ifcowl:IfcCurtainWall.

6.2.4
Building, Storey and Space entities
CCS classes corresponding to building, storey or space entities are defined in a similar
fashion although storey and space instances are not given a class-code in the CCS ontol-
ogy, but only the top-node and class-name. The reason for this is primarily that their is
not a standard class-code for storey and functional-spaces in CCS, but also that these
entities are normally given initials specific to the project. As a result this is done in the
rulesets, where the ifcowl:name_IfcRoot value has been utilized. CCS however have
classes for practical application of spaces, but mapping of these based upon IFC entities
and properties is troublesome. IfcSpace and IfcSpaceType does not include predefined
space-types similar to those defined in CCS. Hence to work around this would mean to
make string-matching of property values, which is error-prone so long as the users do
not have a standardized vocabulary to define each space in the model. Thus the current
solution does not include definitions of the application of spaces based on the lack of
definitions in IFC and standardized vocabulary to define spaces in the model, so that
generic rulesets can be implemented that searches for strings of properties related to
space-type.
6.2.5
Rulesets and triple pattern matching
Following the workflow presented in Figure 6.1 it is important to understand the col-
laboration of the CCS ontology and the rulesets, but also the computing done by the
back-end application. In Figure 6.2 an example highlighting the flow of classifying and
identifying a wall structure with a window and a door is illustrated.
From step 1 to 2 the CCS class, class-codes, topnode, and class-name information
is inferred for each element. In the back-end application a query is executed finding
the class-codes for each element and sorting by the type. This allows for numbering
each instance in a specific category of elements and also to determine the IDs of the
different types within the same category allowing for specification of CCS type IDs. The
computed CCS single-level IDs and type IDs are then utilized in the rulesets and in
the case of Figure 6.2 the ruleset utilized determines the CCS multi-level ID based on
void-filling relations between the wall, its openings and the filling elements.
The primary logic of utilizing rulesets for classification is to seperate IFC entities
covering more than one CCS class. SPARQL functions such as FILTER NOT EXISTS and
MINUS would be appropriate elements for this task. However the Stardog reasoner does
support negation and hence these functions cannot be implemented. Thus the rulesets
relies on triple-pattern matching and string-searching, with the latter especially posing
problems with maintaining correct results from various projects.
In the following the ruleset inferring the CCS multi-level IDs based on void-filling
relations, and other relevant rulesets developed for the purpose of determining CCS codes
and IDs will be presented and discussed. The syntax for writing these rulesets are specific
to the chosen graph-database with OWL2 reasoning capabilities Stardog, however the
logic applied is the IF-THEN syntax, which can be converted to other rule-notation
==PAGE:99==
6.2 Development of CCS ontology and rulesets
81
languages. As mentioned only a subset of the rulesets developed will be presented and
for the full specification of rulesets please refer to Appendix E.
6.2.5.1
Ruleset for CCS Multi-Level ID
The example from Figure 6.2 and more specifically the fourth element in the chain is
driven by the ruleset written in Listing 6.2. CCS multi-level ID describes links between
elements in holistic units and hence more than one relationship types can determine these.
This ruleset utilizes the void-opening-fills relation, but other relations could form a rule
for determining other specific multi-level IDs for other element types. This could be done
using the IfcRelServicesBuildings for IfcSystem relations or the IfcRelAssignsToGroup
for IfcGroup.
The first variable ?ifcrelfill finds all instances of type IfcRelFillsElement and
the associated opening- and filling-element. The filling-element is of interest, since this
corresponds to the element for which the CCS multi-level ID is to be associated. Hence
for this filling-element the computed CCS single-level ID received from the back-end ap-
plication is stored in the variable ?fillingID. Next the associated elements for which
the filling-elements fills the openings are found by finding the instances of IfcRelVoidsE-
lement and the opening-elements (also related to the filling-elements). Thus the variable
?elementwithvoid holds the instances of the elements with the voids. Similarily the
CCS single-level ID is stored in a variable ?elementwithvoidID for the elements with
voids and link to the filling-elements. As the finale step of the IF-part the BIND function
is utilized to the connect the variables holding information of the respective IDs of the
elements with voids and the elements filling the voids. The "-" part represents the
prefix for multi-level IDs in CCS. The output of the BIND function is stored in a new
variable ?multiLevelID. Finally in the THEN-clause the filling-elements bindings are
mapped to the ?multiLevelID bindings using the property defined in the CCS ontology
ccs:multiLevelID. The variables binding specific elements are kept generic on purpose,
since this allows to access and include all elements with voids and fills. This is one of
the main advantageous of implementing the semantic web technologies together with
the relations defined in IFC.
1 # Rule for CCS Multi-Level ID (Sammensat produkt -ID).
2 [] a rule:SPARQLRule ;
3
rule:content """
4 IF {
5
?ifcrelfill a ifcowl:IfcRelFillsElement
;
6
ifcowl:relatingOpeningElement_IfcRelFillsElement ?openingelement ;
7
ifcowl:relatedBuildingElement_IfcRelFillsElement ?fillingelement .
8
9
?fillingelement ccs:singleLevelID ?fillingID
.
10
11
?voidelement a ifcowl:IfcRelVoidsElement ;
12
ifcowl:relatedOpeningElement_IfcRelVoidsElement ?openingelement ;
13
ifcowl:relatingBuildingElement_IfcRelVoidsElement ?elementwithvoid .
14
==PAGE:100==
82
6 Product
15
?elementwithvoid ccs:singleLevelID ?elementwithvoidID .
16
17
BIND (CONCAT("-", ?elementwithvoidID , ".", ?fillingID) AS ?multiLevelID)
18
}
19 THEN {
20
?fillingelement ccs:multiLevelID ?multiLevelID .
21
}""".

6.2.5.2

this code. A similar approach is performed for the spaces with storing the room/space
no. in a variable ?roomno and then using the BIND function together with CONCAT to get
a result that looks like #R1, #R2 etc.

by the IfcRelAggregates.
The CCS single-level IDs of the elements is as previously

mentioned computed by the back-end application, while for the storeys and spaces this

A window with single-level ID #QQA32 placed within Room 116 (#R116) and located on
Storey 4 (#E4) will then have CCS single-level location-at ID called ++E4.R116.QQA32.

6.2.5.4
Ruleset for CCS Functional-ID of elements in IfcSystem
In determining elements as part of a functional whole the CCS functional-ID represents
the hierarchy of elements connected. In this proposal the ruleset developed only func-
tions on two levels namely the overall system and the element included in this system.
IfcSystem covers both distribution- and building-systems among others, and this ruleset
determines the links between elements and a IfcDistributionSystem and creates a CCS
functional-ID for these relations. In reality the rule could be extended to cover all as-
pects of IfcSystem, but this was kept specific to IfcDistributionSystem, since this entity
was investigated thourogly and hence the authors was certain of the outcome. Future
work could investigate this further in order to cover more detailed functional-relations
and also to cover more than just IfcSystem alone. The ruleset is presented in Listing
6.5.

6.2.6
Complex geometries
Classifying complex geometries or proxy-objects is difficult using class-operators or prop-
erty restrictions. As Figure 6.3 illustrates the shaped profile can either be considered
as a wall or a roof extending from top to terrain level. In this case mapping from CCS
to IFC is not easy and requires the user to categorize the proxy-object to an equiva-
lent common element such as wall or roof specific to the chosen BIM application. By
this categorization the user indicates what this proxy-object should be represented as
in CCS and more specifically what IFC entity it should be exported as. Since not all
CCS classes and equivalent IFC elements are included in the CCS ontology a simple
query finding all non-classified elements can give the project-team a sense of what lacks
CCS classification and either manually describe these or designate a common category
to these in the BIM model and hence make use of the definitions in the CCS ontology.
6.2.7
Group of items
With different actors having a perspective of what the design of a building is decomposed
of, having a common understanding during exchanges of data and during design in
digital tools can be challenging. Figure 6.4 represents an example of the perspective of
an architect with regards to a wall in the building and then the actual modelling and
geometric representation of said wall in a BIM model. In this case what the architect
sees as a whole of a wall structure is modelled as two separate wall entities. This could
==PAGE:104==

Figure 6.3: Concept of complex geometries being difficult to distinguish and map to

be a common representation of a brick external wall, which in the BIM tools is modelled
in sections according to the storeys of the building. Using CCS in this case is possible,
but the question is rather which model to follow. A way to mitigate this problem is
developed in the CCS rulesets by considering these walls as a IfcGroup.
Using the
properties related to IfcGroup it is possible to determine whether the group of elements
consists of walls alone and hence the IfcGroup could be categorized as a ccs:Wall and
inherit the values for this class defined in the CCS ontology. Listing 6.6 highlights the
ruleset developed for this purpose. However this leads to other problems, since each of
the wall elements are both handled individually and as a part of the IfcGroup. This
would cause for the numbering of each instance of ccs:Wall to be disrupted, since the
walls would be given a number, while they are not an individual entity but part of a whole
i.e. the IfcGroup. Further programming of scripts for computing these dependencies is
needed, since this is not possible from semantic web technologies alone. Due to time
constraints this was not investigated further.

Following the principles of IfcGroup another limited process of classification is with
regards to CCS ’Window section’ and ’Window’. In Revit families can be created which

should represent a window section consisting of several different window entities. Fol-
lowing the CCS system these window entities should be classified as ’Window’ with
class-code ’QQA’, while the section is classified as ’Window Section’ with class-code
’QQB’. This concept is illustrated in Figure 6.5, where the ideal situation is shown and
also the solution provided by the CCS ontology. Since the window family is treated as a
single entity there is not any link between the whole and the individual parts. Also dur-
ing conversion to IFC the instances of this family will be exported as either IfcWindow
or IfcWindowStandardCase. As a result the mapping from CCS classes to IFC in the
ontology will result in the window family to be a ccs:Window and hence have class-code
’QQA’. Another scenario where the difference between ’Window Section’ and ’Window’ is
not readily accounted for is in simple wall buildups or curtain walls, where the modelling
in the BIM model is intended to geometrically represent window sections, but however
there is not any relation between the individually entities. With regards to semantic
relations it is thus very difficult to automatically register if a group of windows are part
of a window section in function. This is partially a problem with the IFC schema, since
window-sections are not represented in IFC whether it be a family or an actual group of
window entities modelled in a wall system. As previously mentioned with the solution of
implementing a IfcGroup representing multiple wall instances as a single wall-entity, this
could also be utilized in this case, but the same issues with regards to the identification
of window instances is present.
Triple pattern matching and rulesets does function well when keeping the amount
of bindings for each variable infinite, since this does not constraint the rulesets to indi-
vidual object types, but rather includes all types of elements if specified in this manner.
==PAGE:106==
88
6 Product
#QQB1 - Window Section 1
#QQA1 - Window 1
#QQA2 - Window 2

Figure 6.5: Difficulties with classification of a Revit window family with supposed
multiple window instances.
However some entities that could be represented as a IfcGroup, operates with specific
composition and numbers of elements such as for instance a ’Light-box’. Different types
of this entity exists, but in general it consists of three walls and a slab. Modelling this in
Revit and creating a IfcGroup is simple and straightforward. But apart from the issues
with identification, developing a ruleset that infers a common class with regards to a spe-
cific classification-system including CCS is difficult, since this requires a finite number
of triple-patterns to be matched. As shown in Listing 6.6 the link between the IfcGroup
and the entities included is made through the ifcowl:relatedObjects_IfcRelAssigns.
As a result the variable that binds the values of this property includes all elements, and
hence it not straightforward to infer that this group consists of exactly three walls and
a slab. In reality it could be build in numerous ways and still be considered a ’Light-
box’. Hence there are certain challenges with both mapping to a relevant IFC class,
when there is not any, but also in the attempt to reason the class-membership through
IF-THEN statements, when the logic requires finite members. A solution could be to
create a common property to the group with a industry-standard value that could be
matched to through a string-pattern matching, which as shown in previous chapters is
possible in RDF syntax. The main challenge lies in choosing a specific name, which is
understood by project-teams and their software. The triple structure could help with
the process, but this requires further advancement and research in industry-standards
and vocabularies.
==PAGE:107==
6.2 Development of CCS ontology and rulesets
89
6.2.8
Rulesets driven by specific properties
Despite that the previously presented rulesets did utilize properties and their values they
are not however driven by these values. One of the challenges in creating an ontology
that maps between CCS classes and IFC entities is that within a IFC entity many con-
cepts of CCS can be covered. One example of this is with regards to structural elements
such as beams, columns etc. CCS operates with elements above the ground level and ele-
ments specific for foundational purposes. Hence for instance columns are represented as
both ’Column (ULD)’ and ’Foundation-column (UKD)’ representing their placement be-
ing above or below ground. Modelling a column in Revit being it a structural column for
foundational purposes or not, the export to IFC will represent this entity as a IfcColumn-
StandardCase with supertype IfcColumn. The associated IfcColumnType holds common
information of the column instance with ifcowl:predefinedType_IfcColumnType spec-
ified in enumerations. However to determine if a column is ’ULD’ or ’UKD’ based alone
on the IFC export is not readily applicable. Using the IF-THEN rule syntax however
other properties can infer a difference for instance based on elevation-data.
As mentioned previously about reasoning through the ontology and rulesets this
creates challenges. If elements are given a class-code through the ontology, but are then
also given a class-code as a consequence of a ruleset the instance will have two triples
stating these facts respectively. In general this only adds to the knowledge received
by the knowledge graph and gives the end-user a deeper understanding of the building
elements. But with regards to identification this poses problems, since for instance a
column will first get ccs:classCode 'ULD' through the ontology, but is then included
again in the ruleset determining the difference of ordinary columns and foundation-
columns and hence given ccs:classCode 'UKD'. Mitigating these challenges is not a
straightforward process , since the solution is dependent on the logic of the inference-
engine and the syntax of RDF triples.
Stardog does not support non-monotonomic
reasoning i.e. if a fact about an instance has been reasoned through for instance the
definitions in the ontology and then another fact is stated in the rulesets, then both
statements holds true. With regards to a column and a foundation-column this is then
not possible by using the owl:equivalentClass ifcowl:IfcColumn for the defined CCS
class ccs:Column. This is namely due to that the individuals defined by the ontology
includes both columns that are intended to be ordinary columns and then the columns
placed in the foundation. Hence a ruleset defining when a column should be treated
as a CCS foundation-column would then be included both in the ontology and in the
ruleset, and hence as a consequence would be included in the identification process twice
in the back-end application. This is as mentioned before that the back-end application
identifies and numbers instances on the basis of the class-code, which for the majority
of instances is determined based on the CCS ontology.
For these elements, which have a possibility to be mapped to two distinct categories
despite being of the same IFC entity such as IfcColumn, which in CCS can be both ’Col-
umn’ and ’Foundation-column’, the class-definitions in the CCS ontology are modified.
In Listing 6.7 the class-definiton of beams and columns is shown. The main difference
from these definitions compared to the previously demonstrated definitions for walls,
==PAGE:108==
90
6 Product
windows etc. is the fact that an equivalent class relation to an IFC entity is not stated.
Hence from the ontology alone no inviduals in the IFC-to-RDF graph will be linked
to any of these definitions. This method is applied in order to state the membership
through rulesets, where specific properties determines if in this case a column (or beam)
is of class ccs:Column or ccs:FoundationColumn. Listing 6.8 shows the rulesets devel-
oped for determining these relations. These rulesets uses the elevation data associated
with the building-storeys and filters for elevations above or below zero respectively. This
is a loose assumption, since it might not always be the case that elements below ground-
level is members of the foundation-system and furthermore even that elevation levels
below 0 corresponds to ground-level.

In continuation of the discussion ifcowl:predefinedType_Entity and property-
driven classification the IfcCovering entity can be mentioned.
The reason that this
entity is particularily applicable for this purpose is that it by definition covers more
than one distinct entity-type. The types CEILING, FLOORING, and ROOFING are mapped
in IFC-to-RDF to an instance of IfcCovering through the predefined-type relation. Using
triple-pattern matching in three separate rules respective of the three types the different
instances of IfcCovering can be mapped to some CCS relevant class. Listing 6.10 shows
the rules developed for IfcCovering.
1 # Rule for IfcCovering to CCS FloorCovering
2 [] a rule:SPARQLRule ;
3
rule:content """
4

5
?el a ifcowl:IfcCovering ;
6

7
}
8
THEN {
9
?el a ccs:FloorCovering .
10
}""".
11

14
rule:content """
15
IF {
16
?el a ifcowl:IfcCovering ;
17
ifcowl:predefinedType_IfcCovering ifcowl:CEILING .
18
}

THEN {
20
?el a ccs:CeilingCovering .

6.2.9
Mapping to other classification systems
In continuation of property-driven classification the examples of ontology and ruleset
creation for the SfB classification-system has also been developed. The SfB ontology and
rulesets can be found in Appendix F. The examples included are SfB Exterior-wall (21)
and Interior-wall (22) in order to highlight the concept. Figure 6.6 illustrates the triple
pattern in the the IF-THEN ruleset developed for reasoning of SfB interior-wall instances
based upon the IFC definitions. Furthermore mapping between SfB interior-wall and
CCS wall-structure is also included by using owl:sameAs, which shows the potential
of utilizing linked data and semantic definitions. As of know only the function-code is
included in the SfB ontology, but extending it to cover the construction- and resource-
codes requires a more in-depth look and also that generic definitions defining these codes
are available. In general the semantic and linked relations available in the IFC-to-RDF
graph and the triple-pattern matching of rulesets functions well for determining codes.
This is seen both in the development and results of the CCS- and SfB ontologies, whereas
the identification process requires computing beyond SPARQL and RDF.
6.3
Overview of back-end application
The back-end application is developed on the cloud-based developing-environment Cloud9.
The application is built using the Javascript language together with Node-Package Man-
ager (npm). Using npm allows for Javascipt-based code to utilize reusable code called
node packages or modulus (Npm.Inc 2017). These modulus operates as building-blocks
for building an application, webpage or server. One such node module is specifically cre-
ated for accessing Stardog through a hosted server for querying and reasoning. Cloud9
hence also functions as a hosted server with the specific URL for the developed back-end
being https://speciale-arbazhamayun.c9users.io.

6.3.1
Accessing Stardog
In order to access the Stardog server through the back-end a HTTP GET request is per-
formed. In Cloud9 this is performed by the app.get('/',function(req,res,next))
routing, which defines the end-point and the response to the request prompted. Before
the routes are defined and can be executed a number of properties needs to be defined.
First and foremost the node modules necessary needs to be defined as either variables
or constant dependent on whether the elements are expected to change or not. For ac-
cessing Stardog and running the route the respective node modules needed are ’stardog’
and ’express’. The Stardog connection with Cloud9 is defined as a variable conn defined
as new stardog.Connection(). Details specific for Stardog are conn.setReasoning(),
conn.setEndpoint() and conn.setCredentials(username,password). Reasoning is
set to ’true’ and the end-point is specified to be https://speciale-arbazhamayun.
c9users.io:8082, where :8082 represents the port that Stardog runs on. The port
of the host i.e. the back-end server is set as a variable using the following definition
process.env.PORT||8080. Per default the credentials are set to ’admin’ for both user-
name and password, which can be changed during database creation.
==PAGE:113==
6.3 Overview of back-end application
95
6.3.2
Running and sending data to Stardog
Before any database can be created the Stardog server needs to be running. This is done
by the code listed in Listing 6.11, which ends any running Stardog server and starts a
new Stardog server. Running the code the response illustrated in Figure 6.7 is received
if the server has successfully started.
1 End_stardog() {
2
stardog -admin --server http://0.0.0.0:8082 server stop |
3
# Delete lock-file if present
4
if [ -f /data/stardog/system.lock ]; then
5
rm -f /data/stardog/system.lock
6
fi
7 }
8 Start_stardog() {
9
stardog -admin server start --bind 0.0.0.0 --port 8082
10 }
11
12 End_stardog | Start_stardog
Listing 6.11: Code for ending any running Stardog server and starts a new server.
Developed by supervisor Mads Holten Rasmussen.
Figure 6.7: Successful startup of Stardog server.
In order to create a database and send RDF files to the database the following
command is used:
stardog:admin --server http://0.0.0.0:8082 db create -n
databasename file1.rdf file2.rdf…Sending Turtle files is done by specifying this
in the command by file1.ttl.
