6.3.3
Function determining CCS single-level and
type-IDs
Stardog supports the SPARQL syntax for querying data and it is the queries that initi-
ates the reasoning process. As previously mentioned the collaboration between the ontol-
ogy and rulesets with the back-end application drives the determination of classification-
and identification-codes. In Appendix G the full app.get() route and underlying func-
tion developed for determining the CCS single-level- and type-IDs.
The first part in app.get('/:proj/allElements',function(req,res,next) i.e.
'/:proj/allElements' specifies the end-point that should be accessed and queried.
This part is an extension to the already defined URL for the server, so that when the
URL https://speciale-arbazhamayun.c9users.io/:proj/allElements is executed
the Stardog server is accessed and a specified query is executed. Querying data from
Stardog using the ’stardog’ npm follows the syntax listed in Listing 6.12. The :proj
part of the URL indicates the database that is to be queried and hence functions as
a variable that can be changed to any string. This listing also represents the query
developed for the purpose of determining CCS single-level IDs and type-IDs.
1 conn.query({
2
database: proj,
3
query:`SELECT DISTINCT ?uri ?index ?type2 \
4
WHERE { \
5
?uri a ifcowl:IfcProduct . \
6
?uri ccs:classCode ?index . \
7
?uri ifcowl:objectType_IfcObject/express:hasString ?type2 . \
8
} ORDER BY ?index ?type2
9 },function (data) {}
Listing 6.12: SPARQL query initiating the reasoning process in order to infer ccs:
classCode for all elements.
The query in Listing 6.12 infers the ccs:classCode for all elements and orders the
results by these values and the ifcowl:objectType_IfcObject of the instances stored
in the variable ?type2. The query determines all occurrences of ifcowl:IfcProduct and
binds them to the variable ?uri. This procedure is followed since IfcProduct represents
objects related to geometry and spatial contexts, which hence includes building elements
and also spatial-elements such as building, storey and space. The computing of the IDs
is done in the second half of conn.query(query,function{}). The reason for ordering
the data based on the ifcowl:objectType_IfcObject becomes clearer by examining
the processing of the results of the query, which are stored in data.results.bindings.
In the function part of conn.query the code for the computing the IDs is developed.
The function consists of three sections namely (1) a prelimenary phase with definitions
of initial values, (2) a for loop running for the number of bindings stored in variable i
in data.results.bindings in which the IDs are computed, and (3) a post-processing
part where the found IDs are sent back to Stardog and the results are shown in a HTML
table.
==PAGE:115==
6.3 Overview of back-end application
97
Listing 6.13 lists the code of the first section of the function. In order to send data
back to Stardog a Turtle file is created in the constant filename.
The logStream
variable writes strings to this file and in this phase only the prefix part of the file is
written to the Turtle file. Two empty variables tempProduct and tempType are defined
for usage in the for loop. Furthermore two variables countProduct and countType
are created for the purpose of counting the instances of elements and number of types
within an element-category. Initially these are set to 1, since the first occurrence in the
bindings is given identification 1 for single-level ID.
1 function (data) {
2
var tempProduct , tempType;
3
var countProduct = 1, countType = 1;
4
const filename = uuid()+'.ttl'
5
const filename2 = '/home/ubuntu/workspace/server/IFC4_ADD1'+'.ttl'
6
var logStream = fs.createWriteStream(filename , {'flags': 'w'})
7
logStream.write('@prefix ccs: <http://www.student.dtu.dk/~s112449/
ccs#> .\n \n')
Listing 6.13: Prelimenary part of the function.
In the for loop listed in Listing 6.14 the count for single-level IDs and type-IDs
is determined based on the bindings in the variables tempValue and tempTypeValue.
tempValue holds values for the ccs:classCode of instances and since the query is or-
dered by these values the shift and changes of the class-code can be traced. Thus if the
first 10 bindings are for instances defined as ccs:Wall with codes ’AD’ the following
statements determines the identification and furthermore registers to reset the counters
if and when instances of a new class occurs.
The first if statement initially sets the variables defined before the loop to the
first results of the bindings. The values stored in tempProduct is matched with the
corresponding value in tempValue in the following if statement. For each time the
values of tempProduct and tempValue are not equal the count is reset, which ensures
that the following instances of a certain class are numbered accordingly. The last if
statement does a similar operation for determining the type-IDs by matching the shift
of the object-type stored in tempTypeValue.
The computed IDs are then stored in the variables indexValue and typeValue de-
fined as the class-code value + the count. These findings are then written to the Turtle
file as triples using the logStream.write function. This is done by specifying the sub-
ject as the instance uri (inst:) and then using the property classes ccs:singleLevelID
and ccs:typeID to attach the object-values.
From the interview with ALECTIA it was made clear that specific intervals of num-
bers corresponds to identification of different types of elements. The function developed
does not facilitate such definitions primarily due to the lack of predefined types and
string-value based entity description, and furthermore it being more specific than in-
tended for the project. The first point is of importance, since in order to extend and
implement definitions to the entities in the building model requires a semantically rich
profile of such definitions. Since definitions of such types are primarily defined in the
==PAGE:116==
98
6 Product
properties of the elements and not as such a part of the IFC standard, seeking to map
these to a semantic meaning could be difficult. String-based values are not a reliable
source to identify the type of a element in a semantic web context, so long as there is
not a standard for such definitions. IFD and the subsequent bSDD developments would
help to mitigate these challenges as more and more widely-accepted entity declarations
are defined.
1 for (var i in data.results.bindings) {
2
var tempValue = data.results.bindings[i].index.value;
3
var tempTypeValue = data.results.bindings[i].type2.value;
4
if (!tempProduct) { // init values , used as previous values for the
comparison
5
tempProduct = tempValue;
6
tempType = tempTypeValue;
7
}
8
if (tempProduct != tempValue) { // reset counters
9
countProduct = 1;
10
countType = 1;
11
tempType = tempTypeValue;
12
}
13
14
if (tempTypeValue != tempType) { // Updates the type counter
15
countType = parseInt(countType) + 1;
16
}
17
18
var uriValue = data.results.bindings[i].uri.value;
19
var indexValue = tempValue + countProduct;
20
var typeValue = tempValue + countType;
21
22
tempProduct = tempValue;
23
tempType = tempTypeValue;
24
countProduct = parseInt(countProduct) + 1;
25
logStream.write(`<${uriValue}> ccs:singleLevelID "${indexValue}"^^
xsd:string . \n`)
26
logStream.write(`<${uriValue}> ccs:typeID "${typeValue}"^^xsd:string
. \n`)
Listing 6.14: for loop and if statements computing single-level- and type-IDs.
The finale commands executed by the function is first to send the created Turtle file
with triples to Stardog and furthermore to display the computed results on the webpage.
The Stardog data add command is utilized for this purpose as defined in the constant
cmd. The IDs are utilized in the CCS rulesets for determining multi-level-, functional-,
and location-IDs. Furthermore another command is executed cmd2, which removes the
ifcOWL ontology from the project-database on Stardog. The reason for introducing this
command is due to the logic of OWL and RDFS. As previously mentioned subclasses
inherit some definitions of superclasses in OWL logic and with the ontology present
during the execution of rulesets for computing the CCS codes, this could cause for several
incorrect and inapplicable results. Furthermore query and reasoning performance could
be effected by the presence of the ifcOWL ontology. In this solution the ifcOWL ontology
==PAGE:117==
6.3 Overview of back-end application
99
is only used for computing the single-level- and type-IDs by utilizing ifcowl:IfcProduct
in order to find all instances of relevance.
1 Execute command
2 const cmd = `stardog data add ${endpoint}/${proj} ${filename} -u ${username}
-p ${password}`
3 exec(cmd, function(error, stdout , stderr) {
4
console.log(error)
5
console.log(stdout)
6
console.log(stderr)
7
error != null ? res.status(500).send(error) : res.status(200).send(html)
8
})
9
10 const cmd2 = `stardog data remove ${endpoint}/${proj} ${filename2} -u ${
username} -p ${password}`
11 exec(cmd2, function(error, stdout , stderr) {
12
console.log(error)
13
console.log(stdout)
14
console.log(stderr)
15
})
Listing 6.15: Sending Turtle file with computed triples for single-level IDs and type-
IDs back to Stardog.
In practice assigning identification is done once or twice in the initial phases and
then these numbers are referenced throughout the project or deleted or edited. Hence
a window instance ”#QQA2” could be deleted and hence this should be reflected in
the identification of any future window instance modelled i.e. that the next available
identification should be ”#QQA3” and not ”#QQA2” despite it not being utilized in
the project. This is to ensure consistency throughout the project documentation, since
references to these codes would happen in more than one phase of the project and
that these are subjected to changes. The current solution poses an application that
computes the first classification and identification of the building model entities. The
application is limited by the IFC-to-RDF generation tool, since each unique instance is
defined by the inst: namespace prefix, which is based upon the date and time of the
conversion. Furthermore the suffix is generated based on the #identification-number in
the IFC-STEP file, which hence leads to different URIs for the same entity based on
each conversion, since the identification-numbers are subject to change. Thus for each
time the BIM model is changed it needs to first be converted to IFC and then to RDF
and with the current logic of the IFC-to-RDF conversion tool comparing these instances
is not possible due to the uniqueness of URIs. Also the concept of deleted and modified
entities and the corresponding changes in CCS identification-numbers is still a challenge
unsolved by the current product. As mentioned this product determines the running
numbers in a isolated fashion meaning for each converted IFC-to-RDF building model
graph. Future work could implement coding to match triples from different models using
versioning concepts or based upon the IfcGUID values.
==PAGE:118==
100
6 Product
6.3.4
Function determining and writing CCS single-level
IDs for instances of IfcSpatialStructureElement
Out of the developed rulesets the ones determining CCS single-level location-at IDs
requires the highest performance of the inference engine due to the dependencies of
other rulesets. The IDs of spaces and storeys are namely included in these dependencies.
In order to improve the performance of the back-end in combination with Stardog with
regards to inference of location-IDs an initial function is created that writes the results
of the reasoning of ccs:singleLevelID for spaces and storeys back to Stardog for usage
in the said rulesets.
Thus these facts are explicitly stated and the inference engine
does not need to reason these facts, but only performs the reasoning for determining
void/filling-relations, containment in storeys, combining IDs using BIND functions etc.
In Listing 6.16 the query utilized in this regard is listed and the full app.get() route is
found in Appendix H.
1 conn.query({
2
database: proj,
3
query:`select distinct ?uri ?id \
4
where { \
5
?uri a ifcowl:IfcSpatialStructureElement . \
6
?uri ccs:singleLevelID ?id . \
7
}`

//
9

6.3.5
Functions displaying the results of the CCS
classification and identification
With the results for the single-level IDs and type-IDs sent back to Stardog it is possi-
ble to execute a query that gives all possible CCS codes for a single class of elements.
The HTTP GET requst is sent trough two distinct host addresses https://speciale-
arbazhamayun.c9users.io/:proj/buildingelements/:type and https://speciale-
arbazhamayun.c9users.io/:proj/distributionelements/:type. The reason for cre-
ating two functions is that the performance of the Cloud9 server/back-end is significantly
decreased if all CCS codes are to be inferred through one query. As mentioned in the
chapter about the development of the CCS ontology and rulesets the current solution
i.e. the combination of ontology, rulesets and back-end focuses on CCS Single-Level
ID, Type-ID, Multi-Level ID, Multi-Level Type-ID, Single-Level Location-At ID, and
Functional-ID. However there is not necessarily a result for all of these classification
parameters for certain CCS classes. For some elements the rulesets needs to be further
==PAGE:119==
6.3 Overview of back-end application
101
developed in order to include these, and for other elements certain parameters are not
relevant, such as Multi-Level ID for walls or Functional-ID for doors. Thus as a result
the two app.get() routes are created in order to capture the relevant codes for building-
elements such as walls, windows, doors and distribution-elements such as pipe-fittings,
ducts, fans. In order to capture this concept in the query the OPTIONAL pattern match
is implemented. Listing 6.17 presents the SPARQL queries written in the functions for
extracting the results of the CCS classification. As can be seen these routes includes two
variables :proj and :type. The :type defines the building element category that the
user wants to get results for. Thus sending the request to Stardog for a database called
’BuildingA’ and expecting results for ccs:Window instances would yield the following
URL https://speciale-arbazhamayun.c9users.io/BuildingA/buildingelements/
Window. The full two app.get(url,function{}) can be found in Appendix I and J for
distribution-elements and building-elements respectively. Table 6.2 and 6.3 summarizes
the classes of elements supported by the these two back-end functions and the respective
URLs needed to initiate the web-page with results. In reality the two functions supports
both regular building-elements and distribution-elements, but for better performance of
the back-end and the reasoning process separating these is done with the end-user in
mind.

6.4
Sub-conclusion
In order to get from a BIM model, to the elements of the model being automatically
classified and identified in accordance with the CCS system, a product-solution was
developed. The product consists of five main technologies with chosen software namely
(1) a BIM model developed in Autodesk Revit, (2) RDF based graph model of BIM model
created using a IFC-to-RDF conversion tool, (3) development of a CCS ontology and
associated ruleset definitions for querying and reasoning of semantic relations between
the entities, (4) a graph-based triple store named Stardog with querying and reasoning
==PAGE:121==

Table 6.3: Supported CCS classes and URLs for calling the function related to
distribution-elements.
functionality, and (5) a developing-environment functioning as a client-server allowing
for computation and display of results from Stardog.
The CCS ontology and rulesets covers the five categories of CCS coding and class
and element-specific definitions namely CCS top-node, class-code, class-name, single-
level ID, type-ID, multi-level ID, multi-level type-ID, single-level location-at ID, and
functional-ID. The ontology definitions are based on two of the three levels of CCS
element-definition namely the technical systems and components. In order to maintain
proper identification this was a necessary approach, since CCS includes definitions for the
same category of elements in both the functional- and technical systems. IFC exported
models usually include one or maybe two distinct cases of the same category-elements,
which provides challenges with mapping to a CCS class and maintain both proper clas-
sification and identification. Hence the approach suggested allows for mapping of one
CCS entity to one or more distinct IFC entities, but does not facilitate mapping of one
IFC entity to several CCS classes. The CCS ontology and rulesets collaborates with the
rulesets, controlling and creating multi-IDs such as CCS multi-level ID or functional-ID
based on the semantic relations within the IFC format. This however does put some
responsibility on the end-user to properly model and map the chosen families in the
BIM model to an equivalent IFC entity. Complex geometries and proxy-elements can
be mapped based on property-values using rules, but this is not sustainable without
a properly defined vocabulary, since different actors and disciplines have a different
understanding of building entities.
In the Cloud9 back-end application functions were created in order to compute the
IDs for reference in the rulesets. Four distinct URL requests were formed as a result.
The algorithm of the computation of IDs does not include project-specific numbering
as was the case at ALECTIA and moreover maintenance of the IDs in synchronization
with the building-model. This does leave some practical scenarios unsolved, but further
coding and function-development could mitigate these challenges. However RDF and
SPARQL as such does not provide readily available tools for identification-processes and
hence programming-tools are needed. The chosen conversion-tool’s logic for creation of
URIs for entities in the model, was mainly the reason for the maintenance of the IDs
not being properly implemented.
==PAGE:122==
104
==PAGE:123==
CHAPTER 7
Practical Application
In this section of the chapter a practical application of the proposed product is presented.
This demo will highlight how the end-user will get from Revit to a results page with
CCS codes. Furthermore the practical implications and limitations of the chosen steps
within the solution will be discussed throughout.
The initial thought behind the practical application was to utilize a industry-model
developed for actual construction with classification and identification already conducted.
Thus a comparison between the CCS classification and identification of the specific
project model and the solution developed by the authors could lead to a deeper under-
standing of consistency and differences. However due to the performance-limitations
of the Cloud9 server during the testing of the above mentioned approach this was not
possible, since querying and especially reasoning was too heavy for the back-end with
the numerous entities involved. As a result the authors developed a building-model with
a linked systems model for proof of concept and practical application.
7.1
Building Model and export to IFC
7.1.1
Model development
Initially a BIM model is to be developed in Revit 2017 and for the purpose of the demo
this model is kept simple and thus consists of a structural/architectural model and a
linked systems model representing the ventilation-system. Figure 7.1 and 7.2 shows 3D
views of the structural/architectural model and the systems model respectively. For
reference two multi-category schedules is created in order to get an overview of the
number of entities and types within each category of elements. In Table 7.1 and 7.2
these results are summarized for the architectural/structural model and systems model
respectively.
7.1.2
Mapping of families to IFC
Since the product ontology and rulesets were created with IFC4 in mind the export
to IFC is done by using the ’IFC4 Design Transfer View’, since this would allow for
including most entities. While modelling it should be ensured that the families utilized
are categorized properly, since this would lead to inconsistencies when mapped to the

IfcOutlet as anticipated. Further investigation showed that the family utilized was a
generic model and hence the export yielded in a proxy-element. In order to mitigate
such issues a manual process of mapping families to a relevant IFC entity is needed. This
is done by creating the shared parameter ’IFCExportAs’ and modifying the family in
the ’Family Types’ dialog. For this purpose the Revit LT ’IFC Shared Parameters.txt’
file is utilized (Autodesk 2011).
In the Family Editor under properties panel the ’Family Types’ window is opened
and a new parameter is added. The added parameter is set to be a shared parameter
and in the ’Select…’ window the ’IfcExportAs’ is chosen as shown in Figure 7.3. This
parameter is then grouped under ’IFC Parameters’ for ’type’ parameter. In the ’Family
Types’ window a value can be specified for the shared parameter, which for this family
is set to IfcOutlet. Now returning to the model the elements of this family are to be
converted to ’IfcOutlet’ as specified in ’Type Parameters’, which is shown in Figure 7.4.
7.1.3
Export to IFC
Next the model is exported using the ’IFC4 Design Transfer View’ MVD. In the ’Modify
setup…’ window under the ’Additional Content’ tab the ’Export linked files as separate
IFCs’ option is selected. This ensures that both the main-model and linked model are
converted and stored in different IFC files. Furthermore the ’Export Revit property sets’
is also selected, which although gives a information from non-IFC sources still enhances
the semantic knowledge available in the model.

Extracting results for ccs:Window instances utilizing Request 3 yields the results seen
in Figure 7.12. The extracted results shows that the back-end functions has determined
that for the categories of windows the type-IDs are %QQA, %QQA2 and %QQA3. This is in
accordance with the overview presented in Table 7.1. Furthermore the last index of the
single-level IDs is 35, which also in agreement with the model. From this results for CCS
single-level location-at ID it can be seen that for windows this is based on the storey.
This is due to the logic of windows and the associated properties in IFC. The windows
are not seen as contained within a space and thus the IfcRelContainedInSpatialStructure
with the ’relatingStructure’ being an ifcowl:Space does not relate to these instances.
Entities such as CCS Outlets or Furniture does however have a location-ID that includes
the definition of the space, which the element is contained in.
Utilizing Request 4 for querying results for ccs:Duct instances the results presented
in Figure 7.10 are retrieved. As is clear some of the entities do not have a result for
CCS functional-ID. These are the ducts of type ’Taps’ primarily although two instances
of this type does have a value for CCS functional-ID. In order to check if the relations
stated here are correct the IFC model for systems is opened in Revit. From the retrieved
results of the ducts instances two instances are located namely one with no value for
CCS functional-ID and one with. The elements are highlighted in the BIM model as
shown in Figure 7.11. Looking at the function and placement of these elements it would
be fair to assume that the classification is correct, since the element to the right is part
of the modelled ’Airfoil Fan’. However in a practical sense this element should probably
be a part of the system. Moreover with explicitly stated relations between the duct,
airfoil fan and ventilation-system in general the CCS functional-ID results would create
greater value, since the hierarchy of the code would go from functional/technical system
to component (airfoil fan) to specific component (duct).

Including components and specific entities in being part of the IfcSystem should be
investigated next for these purposes. Moreover extending the components of the sys-
tem with explicit attributes of importance to specific domains, would allow for creating
SPARQL queries extracting results of importance to the discipline. Thus the results ex-
tracted for a space and including elements would be different for the ventilation-engineer
compared to the electrical-engineer or fire-design-engineer.

7.5 Potentials and limitations
117
7.5
Potentials and limitations
The proposed solution utilizes the semantic profile of the IFC model by extending and
modifying its properties via the ontology and rulesets to create a semi-automatic classi-
fication and identification system. This however requires for the modelling in the BIM
tool to be controlled with regards to the export to IFC, since any inconsistencies with
the definitions in the CCS ontology would lead to incorrect or no results. The SfB on-
tology created could readily be sent to the Stardog database and hence create a link to
the CCS ontology. This is done again using the Stardog ’data add’ command and in
the Stardog web-console the query listed in Listing 7.2 is executed in order to extract
’External walls’ with regards to both SfB and CCS. Figure 7.13 shows the results of the
query. Creating such links could potentially scale the concept of the solution to cover a
wide variety of classification systems.
1 select ?wall ?SfBfunction ?CCSsinglelevelID
2 where {
3
?wall a sfb:ExteriorWall ;
4
sfb:function ?SfBfunction ;
5
ccs:singleLevelID ?CCSsinglelevelID .}
Listing 7.2: Query executed in Stardog web-console for extracting ’External walls’ and
their definitions in SfB and CCS.
Figure 7.13: Results of query listed in Listing 7.2.
As the demo has highlighted the current setup includes steps, which in practical
design- and collaboration would create inconsistencies within the created data. Here
especially the availability of conversion-tools, the logic behind these and the synchro-
nization between the BIM model and graph-based software are the primary elements
that needs further optimization. Developments in semantic-web tools for AEC projects
is still being researched and has yet to see real industry implementation, and hence so
far most of the software out there are created with the purpose of research. The chosen
conversion-tool might not allow for easy maintenance of entity definitions or change
==PAGE:136==
118
7 Practical Application
management, but it has brought forth the necessary definitions, by which a product
has been developed. This product has proven to be a automatic CCS classification and
identification application although the utilization of the product requires to follow a
semi-automatic workflow.
==PAGE:137==
CHAPTER 8
Discussion
In this chapter of the paper the project results and experiences will be discussed and
this will lead to a holistic reflection of the practical possibilities and limitations of the
implementation of Linked Data and Semantic Web in relation to BIM, classification and
AEC projects in general.
Of the main benefits of utilizing Linked Data and Semantic Web technologies is the
triple-structure of RDF and logic of OWL. With regards to the developed CCS ontology,
properties and classes were defined by simple RDF and OWL definitions. The triple-
structure of the data within the converted IFC-to-RDF made decoupling data easy and
the semantic relations between entities was also easier to grasp and to extract for usage
and computation. While the CCS ontology was developed with the intent of holding
information of CCS class-code, top-node and name, the rulesets were an integrate part
of creating the multi-level IDs such as CCS multi-level ID, single-level location-at ID and
functional-ID. The IF-THEN structure of rules provided useful triple-pattern matching
capabilities together with SPARQL functions such as BIND, FILTER etc. Furthermore
extending the classification to other systems such as SfB and mapping between these
was also possible using the chosen technologies. The determination of class-codes and
membership through reasoning and ontologies was of the main advantageous of utilizing
semantic web technologies. Due to the triple-structure of RDF and it not being a list-
structure the identification process is not readily applicable to be determined through
semantic web tools. This required further computational sources such as JavaScript,
which was the chosen coding-language utilized in the project.
In general the 1-1 mapping of CCS classes to equivalent IFC entities using owl:
equivalentClass was an acceptable solution, but defining all CCS classes using this
approach was problematic and hence some CCS classes were omitted, which was also the
primary reason for only including the technical systems and components in the product.
CCS has many distinct classes, which describes the type of element at the core. IFC
concerns itself with data exchange and interoperability and hence the entity declarations
in IFC are not as extensive as the number of entities in CCS. Thus mapping of several
distinct CCS classes to a corresponding available entity in IFC would yield that the
elements of the building model belongs to more than one CCS class, which would be in
violation of the intended use of CCS and troublesome for the identification process. This
concept was experienced a multitude of times during the project and one example of this
was with the CCS classes ”Window-section (QQB)” and ”Window (QQA)”. In order to
extend the possibilities of mapping, utilization of other AEC developed ontologies could
==PAGE:138==
120
8 Discussion
be implemented. However this poses problems with creating the IFC-to-RDF model and
hence mapping of IFC entities to these ontologies similar to the issues with the mapping
of CCS.
The BIM model provided information of elements with a multitude of properties orig-
inating from the native BIM software and also from IFC. Therefore some of the entities,
which were not mapped with owl:equivalentClass in the CCS ontology, were sought
to be mapped on the basis of element properties. This required the use of IF-THEN
statements for reasoning, which did provide the triple-pattern and string-matching ca-
pabilities that would allow for inferring CCS class-membership on the basis of property
values. However actually creating rulesets yielding correct results was not straightfor-
ward primarily due to (1) the triple-structure of RDF, (2) the Stardog ruleset reasoner
not supporting negation i.e. FILTER NOT EXISTS and MINUS functions, and (3) the risks
involved with string-matching due to non-standardized property values. The latter was
experienced with attempting to properly define CCS equivalent classes of proxy’s and
spaces. IfcProxy instances are defined by their name and associated properties, but in
order to capture this in a ruleset using string-search matching is not a sustainable so-
lution. Since language-barriers exists and different disciplines might look at an object
differently the name of the proxy-element cannot be the determining factor for mapping.
Although not implemented in the developed product it is proposed to give the end-user
an overview over proxy instances and allow the user to map these instances to a relevant
CCS class. Similarly the classification of spaces was also limited by the types associated
with IfcSpace, which were of limited variety. Again by searching for specific string value
of properties a standardized vocabulary or common wording of these values needs to be
in place before it can be ensured that the results are valid.
In addition to the above mentioned, a BIM model is at the core a geometrical model
with attachment of properties, and hence the information stored in the RDF database
related to geometrical data could be investigated further and included as a part of the
solution. This could be a different approach to determine the internal relations between
elements and perhaps be a more precise solution for characterizing when elements are
in systems and isolation. The current definitions of geometrical 3D IFC data in RDF
requires a high-level of understanding of list-structures and does not seem readily avail-
able for application.
The proposed product allows the end-user to go from IFC to RDF and then using
the back-end application and routes to retrieve results relevant to CCS of both building-
and distribution-elements. This current workflow does include some static steps, where
the end-user must take active steps requiring an understanding of running Stardog and
how to create and send data to a Stardog database. Some of steps could be made more
user-friendly by including these as a part of the interface.
Of the main limitations with regards to the practical application of the product is
the identification process. Elements are prone to be changed and modified during design.
ALECTIA utilized their CCS classification and identification of elements in documen-
tation regarding work-description, cost-estimation and quantity-takeoff. Therefore it is
==PAGE:139==
8 Discussion
121
of importance that references made in the documentation reflects the actual modelling
within the model. This is also in continuation of the logic of determining IDs, since
deleting elements and inserting new ones should be reflected in the numbering of CCS
codes. The IFC-to-RDF conversion tool developed by Pauwels and Oraskeri (2015) con-
structs the URIs of resources based on their placement within the IFC-STEP file, which
hence causes the same element to be defined differently from one conversion to another.
A more appropriate approach could be to utilize the IFC GUID values for construct-
ing the URIs of elements. Not all entities of the model are given GUID values, but
since the scope of this thesis and CCS in general is classification and identification of
building-elements, the majority of relevant entities would have a GUID value. However
since GUID values are changed when elements are deleted and new ones are inserted
this approach would have difficulties with ensuring data consistency between the codes,
identification number, and documents referring to these. With cloud-based application
engineering becoming an integrate part of many software including Autodesk Revit the
potential of developing semantic web tools for classification with a more controlled envi-
ronment for maintaining data consistency, global uniqueness of URIs and management
of changes could be the next steps of the development.
Utilizing the triple-structure of RDF and the logic of OWL, a more well-defined
information model is available. Furthermore the links between the attributes and prop-
erties of IFC entities were semantically richer and allowed for mapping between relevant
entities of CCS and IFC, although it not being applicable for all CCS classes, which
hence required the ruleset development. Furthermore the semantic profile of the build-
ing model made defining the multi-level IDs in an automated fashion easy. However
scaling the usage of ifcOWL and IFC-to-RDF with a standardized vocabulary would
allow for even better mapping both with regards to CCS, but also to other building-
domain ontologies. Initiatives such as bsDD could if provided as an OWL ontology be a
step towards the solution of better data exchanges and easier classification. This would
however require that IFC implements the dictionary in its standard and that software-
vendors such as Autodesk develop their BIM software to include such definitions. Thus
it would possible for the designers to better define elements, spaces and proxy-elements,
and even more elements of complex geometry and ambiguity. Such vocabulary defini-
tions if provided in RDF and OWL format could be utilized internationally together
with IFC, due to the possibilities of limiting language barriers of and extending the def-
initions of the subjects, predicates and objects. With such extension possibilities future
work would include investigating how to create ontologies for domain-specific utilization,
by which different domains could extract results for the same element, but only retrieve
information relevant for the tasks within the given domain.
==PAGE:140==
122
==PAGE:141==
CHAPTER 9
Conclusion
The purpose of this thesis was to investigate and answer the following problem statement:
How can Linked Data and Semantic Web technologies be implemented and utilized for
more sustainable data representation allowing for better collaboration in AEC projects?
How can CCS be represented as an equivalent ontology? How can the utilization of the
CCS ontology in combination with ifcOWL automate the classification and identification
of BIM models?
Based on a literature study of Linked Data and Semantic Web technologies it was found
that the semantic web format called RDF with its triple-structure and logic-based OWL
representation would allow for a more explicit, unambiguous and related representation
of data within the AEC domain.
Through a qualitative survey with ALECTIA the practical application and challenges
with classification systems in general and more specifically with CCS was investigated.
Here it was found that the main challenge was to ensure consistency of the classification
data due to the iterative nature of design. This required a lot of resources and time,
since their current work-flow partially was based on manual assignment of CCS.
In order to optimize such work-flows a product was developed on the basis of:
• Developing a CCS ontology by defining a subset of the CCS classes utilizing the
RDF syntax and OWL logic
• Developing rulesets specific for CCS mapping and determination of CCS multi-
level-, location-, and functional IDs utilizing the semantic relations within IFC
• Implementing a converted ifcOWL-based IFC graph-model together with the CCS
ontology and rulesets in a graph-database for querying and reasoning of CCS
entities
• Developing a back-end application server collaborating with the graph-database
for automatic computing of CCS classification and identification
Getting from an IFC model to results for the six CCS identification-codes was made
possible by the developed product. Since the solution was based on the ifcOWL-based
graph-model, defining all of CCS defined entities in the CCS ontology and mapping to
relevant IFC entities was problematic. IFC holds core entity declarations, but however
==PAGE:142==
124
9 Conclusion
does not include specific types or specialized element-categories. Therefore only a sub-
set of the entities defined as technical systems and components in CCS was included in
the project in order to maintain proper identification. Furthermore complex geometries,
group of elements and proxy-elements, which would have a definition in CCS could not
be mapped to equivalent IFC entities at face-value.
We believe that extending the definitions within BIM-software and the IFC standard
with domain-specific ontologies defined as linked data, could yield benefits for the AEC
industry beyond just classification. This however calls for standardization of work-flows
and software-tools providing sustainable design-environment facilitating both domain-
specific tasks, but also collaboration with other domains. With Linked Data and Seman-
tic Web technologies data and information could potentially be available and stored on
the web. This could allow for the wide-variety of information sources to be linked in
specific-projects and utilized for increasing information representation, facilitate a better
foundation for design, and create a sustainable collaboration environment.

==PAGE:149==
APPENDIX A
Interview Guide
A.1
Stage 1
• What is your educational background?
• How many years of experience do you have in the AEC-industry?
• What is your position?
• What are your key responsibilities?
A.2
Stage 2
• What is the goal of implementing CCS for Alectia?
• What about other classification/identification systems, such as IFC, COBie, Om-
niClass, BIM7AA etc.?
• What is CCS used for?
• When in the project-lifecycle is CCS implemented?
• Which software-tools are commonly used for this purpose?
• How do you classify with CCS?
A.3
Stage 3
• Is there any difference in how CCS is used dependent on the given phases of the
project or on the type of project?
• Is there any requirements for sharing CCS classified information, such as content,
formats, quality and time constraints?
• How do you coordinate with other disciplines?
• How do you handle types? Does it differ from one project to another?
• Is there any challenges related to the usage of CCS with regards to identification
procedure, coordination of disciplines etc.?
==PAGE:150==
132
A Interview Guide
A.4
Stage 4
• Is there any room for improvement and where?
• Which technologies could improve or automate the use of CCS?
• Do you see a potential in applying Semantic Web principles in the implementation
of CCS and in the usage of BIM in general?

==PAGE:153==
APPENDIX C
Interview - Transcription
• I: Interviewer
• A: Asger Wahlquist Petersen
A: Uddannelse: Bygningskonstruktør. 3 firmaer. Ansvarlig for at sætte et revit-
API op så der var en sammenhæng mellem entreprenørvirksomheden og modulbyggeri-
produktionen som foregik i en fabrik i Letland. Det jo så 7 år siden, hvor vi begyndte
at snakke om mængder i bygningsmodeller.. Der var tanken, at man skulle trykke på en
knap og så sendte man direkte til vores leverandør. De fik en ordre automatiks. Man
skulle ikke gøre mere end det der stod i modellen. Kæmpe set-up. Mega fedt. Så var
jeg så heldig at komme til Rambøll, hvor jeg var med til at stå for udbudsmateriale og
mængder til det nye fængsel.
I: ”Hvilke ansvarsområder har du?”
A: ”Det er primært Con jeg beskæftiger mig med.”
I: ”Hvor lang tid har du været her og hvad hedder din stilling?”
A: ”1,5 år ; BIM supervisor”
I: ”Hvilke andre klassifikarioner bruger i”
A: ”Vi bruger sfB og vi bruger BIM7AA og så bliver der også stillet krav til det her
forvaltningsklassifikationssystem. Det er dem med CCS, som vi beskæftiger vi os med.
Intet DBK da det er udgået. ”
I: ”Hvornår kommer CCS første gang ind i billedet under et projekt?”
A: ”Vi begynder at overveje det i projektforslaget. Hvordan vores tilbudsliste skal
struktureres og hvordan vores udbudsmateriale i fht beskriverlser. Bagefter kan vi tage
stilling til hvordan tingene skal modelleres. Først der CCS koderne kommer i spil. Men
de er for alvor i spil i hovedprojektet – det er først der, at vi begynder at lav KS af
tingene inde i vores bygningsmodeller.”
I: ”Hvordan gør i det?”
A: ”Vi laver et udtræk til tilbudslisten og sammenholder det med det man har en
forventing om kommer ud. Der er måske nogle ting der mangler koder. Vi sidder også
nogle gange med den fagansvarlige og giver elementerne forskellige farver, så når man
trykker på noget i en schedule. Stemmer det så overens med det man har i modellen.
Men det er ikke mit område, det har jeg ikke overblik over som sådan. Om det lige
netop er den søjle der har den og den kode osv. ”
I:”Sker det her så manuelt?”
A: ”Ja, det er en manuel proces. Man sørger for, at man har modelleret dem i det
rigtige objekt.”
==PAGE:154==
136
C Interview - Transcription
I: ”Hvem tager stilling til de forskellige objekter og typer?”
A: ”Det gør dem der sidder og modellerer, men den fagansvarlige, som sidder med
struktturen til tilbudslisterne kommer med inputs til den der sidder og modellerer”
I: ”Hvis der nu er en type, der ikke eksisterer, hvordan forholder i jer sp til det?”
A: ”Så er det den tekniske assistent der opretter den type, hvis der er noget der
mangler”
I: ”Og det gør han bare sådan?”
A: ”Ja, desværre, der er ikke noget procedure lige for den del.”
I: ”Hvis ting ændrer sig undervejs, er det så bare om at starte om igen? Eksempelvis,
hvis en ting ændrer lokation fra et sted til et andet”
A: ”Placerings-id’et har vi slet ikke med i vores koder. Det har vi ikke behov for og vi
har ikke haft nogle bygherrer som stille krav dertil. Det er ikke noget vi har beskæftiget
os med.”
I: ”Hvilke af de 5 typer arbejder i da med?”
A: ”Vi bruger typen, primært. Og så laver vi noget kombination i MultiLevelTypeID,
men det er i henhold til vores beskrivelser. Vi bruger selvfølgelig og klassifikationskoden,
men den siger meget lidt om selve objektet.”
I: ”Hvilke software benyttes i til klassifikation?”
A: ”Vi har kigget en del på Spine og classify, men det er ikke noget vi bruger. Grunden
er, at det er ganske lidt det kan ift hvad vi selv skal kunne. Den giver egentlig bare at
give den en klassekode, og det er ret simpelt at komme på objektet selv.”
I: ”Hvad med projektets størrelse? Har det en indflydelse på brugen af CCS”
A: ”Primært fast struktur i Alectia. Vi arbejder med to modeller hvor vi klassificere
den enkelte bygningsdel. Vha tyoe. Andre gange har vi prøvet at se om vi har kunnet
holde os fra det, hvis det havde været en mindre projekt – sådan så vi faktisk bare har
defineret at netop disse objekter er defineret i den her bygningsdelsbeskrivelse og så har
vi ikke været helt nede på typeniveau og heller ikke klassifikationsnøglen med de tre
bogstaver.”
I: ”Hvordan koordinerer i med andre discipliner fra andre virksomheder – stiller i
også krav til dem om brugen af CCS?”
A: ”Det kommer an på hvem der har projekteringsledelsen og hvem der har ikt-
ledelsen. Det er typisk arkitektens ansvar. De laver retningslinjerne for hvordan vi skal
strukturere vores ting. Det er også derfor det kan være svært for os at sige vi gør lige
sådan her – fordi forskellige arkitekter arbejder på forskellige måder og det er ikke altid
nemt at sige, at det er lige SÅDAN her vi gør altid. Men hvis det er os der har teten så
vil vi meget gerne prøve at gøre det ensartet.”
I: ”Har du oplevet projekter, hvor det ikke var et krav, at i skulle bruge CCS, men
at i alligevel har gjort det?”
A: ”Nej, ikke endnu. Fordi Alectias standard klassifikations set-up er faktisk baseret
på SfB, men vi er i gang med at konvertere, så vi kan bruge CCS som standard klassi-
fikationssystem. For vi bliver ramt af det med de nye IKT aftaler, hvor kravet er CCS
i model B. I model A er det op til den enkelte rådgiver, hvor det her er oplagt også at
benytte CCS – for hvis det alligevel er et krav i B’eren, hvorfor så ikke bare benytte det
i A’eren også. Det vil gøre tingene nemmere for ALECTIA.”
==PAGE:155==
C Interview - Transcription
137
I: ”Har du nogensinde været med på projekter, hvor det ikke har været et krav, at
man skal bruge CCS?”
A: ”Ja, det er ofte.
Men jeg vil sige, at det er begyndt at blive mere og mere
almindeligt at bruge CCS - også med de nye IKT-bekendtgørelser. Men oftest bliver der
bare skrevet, at der skal være sammenhæng i ens projekt og at man bare skal finde et
passende klassifikationssytem”
I: ”Ser du plads til forbedringer i denne proces med klassifikation af modeller?”
A: ”Der er nok ikke så meget plads til forbedring at hente ved den måde vi gør det
på i ALECTIA. Vi leger lidt med denne her bygningsdatabase. Der hvor jeg ser, at der
er plads til mest forbedring vil være det her placerings-ID. Der ser jeg mest gevinst. Der
hvor det er et helvede for os i øjeblikket er, hvor vi er på store sager, hvor der er en
del Revit-filer, som indgår. Og vi skal sørge for, at de er klassificeret ens på tværs af
de her Revit-filer som vi i dag har. Løbenumre bliver givet ukoordineret og det giver
mange duplikationer. Derfor kigger vi en del på en bygningsdelsdatabse. Det tror jeg
vil hjælpe en del på strukturen.”
I: ”Bliver i nogensinde spurgt efter at anvende lokationsID’er?”
A: ”Nej, det er vi ikke blevet gjort endnu, men jeg kan sagtens forestille mig, at det
kommer i fremtiden. Og der er vi på bar mark i øjeblikket”
I: ”Bruger i CCS i hvert projekt?”
A: ”Nej, det er meget afhængigt af hvem det er der er bygherre og hvem der er
kunde på projektet. Det kan være de har.. Eks KU, de plejer gerne at bruge BIM7aa
som klassifikation system. Nogle bruger CSS – det er meget forskelligt. Men i de nye
IKT-aftaler er der krav om at bruge CCS. Hvis man altså vælger den her Model P. Det er
egentlig ret simpelt. Hvis man bruger Model A eller Model B så vil det mere eller mindre
være et krav, at man bruger CCS som klassifikation. Vi bruger CCS til to ting. Det er
fordi det er ikravsat. Og så bruger vi det til at holde styr på vores udbudsmateriale så
dvs der er sammenhæng mellem beskrivelser, tilbudslister og model. Der bruger vi CCS.
Vi har meegt lidt med driften at gøre. Det er sjældent. Medmindre det er et krav fra
bygherren. Så vi bruger ikke så meget den her placeringhistorik man kan i CCS. Den
del bruger vi ikke på nuværende tidspunkt.”
